		<shortDescription>What can go wrong, will go wrong</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The story of this Coen Brothers film resembles that of 'Fargo': man in need of cash schemes plot; plot fails; man ends up being arrested. In this case he is executed in the electric chair. That serves him right, for people died thanks to his wrong doing. But is he a criminal? By definition, yes. Though to the viewer he is more like a decent civilian who was tempted to take the wrong path and can't go back. Even when he tells the truth -- while his wife is serving in jail for the murder he more or less accidentally committed -- he is not believed by the classy lawyer he has hired. His wife hangs herself, so the case is dismissed. After he is linked to another death, he lands in jail and in afore mentioned chair. He is to die for something he is not guilty of, but accepts the verdict to pay his debts. The film is in black and white which adds to the 'film noir'-character. Note that 'The man who wasn't there' is not the first Coen Brothers film in which hair or hair treatment plays a major role. In 'O Brother, where art thou' there is this crook who is more concerned about his hair than about his general well being (and that of his confr?res). Here the man is a barber in a small town barber shop. Before the execution one of his legs is shaved, a scene that in a macabre way mirrors the bath tub scene in which as a loving husband he shaves his wife's legs. A typical Coen Brothers film with great playing by Billy Bob Thornton. And what a smoker he is! Do the Coen Brothers know the 'sadistic universe' of Dutch literary writer Willem Frederik Hermans? Same as in their films, in his books anything that can go wrong, will go wrong. It is Murphy's Law, as well as Coen's and Hermans'. It would be great to see a Coen Brothers adaption of Hermans' 1966 novel 'Never sleep again'.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Don't underestimate this movie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The use of light and the black and white cinematography in this movie is cool. The cast of this movie is spectacular ant the acting is top notch. The noir homage and period production is dead on.The real genius, though, is the story.....SPOILERSWhy is the name of the movie titled "The Man who wasn't there"? Some would argue that Crane was a typical noir louse, constantly run over by others, who melted into the woodwork, hence the title. I would argue that most of the Crane we see in this movie is made up by Crane the narrator, the Pulp Writer. He really wasn't there.The first time I thought this was in thinking about the name Freddy Riedenschneider and the way Crane was introduced to it by a drunk Mr. Abdubus. Doesn't the name Freddy Riedenschneider sound awfully like "Fred, Ried &amp; Schneider", a more lawyerish name? Perhaps Freddy Riedenschndider was made up by Crane the Pulp Writer. The cool Heisenberg speech sounds awfully like something "written" for the lawyer. I think Freddy was entirely a figment of Crane's imagination. Note the way the switch of lawyers is handled in the editing at Crane's trial.Why did Freddy recommend a guilty plea to the wife to prevent a death sentence and then later Crane receive the death sentence after pleading guilty? Perhaps this "discrepancy" points to one or both of the engagements with the legal system being fiction.Why is the UFO thread in there? The late forties, early fifties were the UFO heyday. Dreams and stories are often filled with random ideas culled from daily life. Notice the sequence of stories in the magazine articles he was reading, first Dry Cleaning then Roswell UFOs. Crane added the UFO thread to the pulp magazine story for some spice but left them subdued or had his character reject abduction because he was contracted to tell his "story", which had to end with execution. As others here have suggested as a mistake could be more proof, CA doesn't use the electric chair. Perhaps Crane was executed with lethal injection or otherwise but told the story with an electric chair to make it more "Sci-fi". Why is the electric chair sequence so white and dreamlike? Along with the implied oral advances by Birdy and Mr. Abdubus's lax approach to Crane being with his daughter, these are examples of Crane's implausible "written" fiction.Another moment in the film that indicates fiction over reality is in the hotel room with Tolliver. The homosexual advance by Tolliver seems forced, subdued, hard to grasp by the viewer. I can't explain it. It took me three times watching it to even notice Tolliver's tongue. Perhaps this is the first attempt by Crane at adding fictitious elements of the story?At first I thought Crane was executed for the death of Big Dave. The complete transition from truth to fiction could be somewhere around the killing of Dave. Crane made the fiction up for the pulp magazine sensabilities. Notice the covers of the magazine he is writing for. Certainly something to consider based on the use of noir "narration".After awhile thinking this was the intent, I revisted the choking scene. Perhaps Crane dies at this point in the movie. You could interpret the entire narration and magazine writing as bogus, just part of Crane's death dream while Big Dave is killing him. The name of the movie would be even more applicable in this interpretation.This movie gives me that "Usual Suspects" or "Mulholland Dr." feeling. What part of the "story" is real? Maybe it was entirely made up.END OF SPOILERSEither way, the Coen Brothers are unparalleled in their lush originality and movie craft.</fullReview>

You'll want to know what happens next, it'll keep you wanting -- unfortunately long past the end credits. And that's where the movie fails. While no one gets off scott free -- classic noir and I loved it-- the Coens just never explain why. Why does Ed Crane let this happen and do nothing. I suppose we are supposed to connect the dots from the title, but give us more to work with IN the film.All the actors were really good in this film. Thorton was perfect in his turn as quiet barber Ed Crane. Frances McDormand was good in her role as Ed's distant wife. I know the Coen's always use her (she's Joel's wife or Ethan's...I can't remember), but she almost always delivers for them. James Gandolfini was excellent as the wife's boss at the department store. But the real standout was Tony Shaloub as a high-price, win-at-all costs attorney. His role was a real gem and he sparkled.The film was never really heavy-handed like film noir can be. The Coens kept it light by throwing some gags in from time to time. And it was a fun movie to watch, but it wasn't a great film. To close, as I said before I found myself wondering about the film's imagery. So what was the meaning of the shadow from the bars cast on Tony Shaloub's character during one of the interview scenes in the interrogation room?</fullReview>

		<date>2010-09-24</date>
		<username>rooprect</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A philosopher's dream</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is staggering. The only reason why I'm not giving it a 10 is that I never give out 10s upon first viewing.If you like movies that challenge your understanding of life, existence and the universe (such as "2001: A Space Odyssey" or "Rashmon" or "Wings of Desire"), you need to check out this film immediately. Don't even finish reading this review. Just watch the movie already.In much the same way the Coen kids did "O Brother Where Art Thou" based on Homer's "The Odyssey", now we have "The Man Who Wasn't There" which--though unconfirmed--has very strong similarities to Albert Camus' "The Stranger". If you haven't read the latter, it's a landmark novel published in 1942, practically defining the Existentialist philosophy. It tells a story of a misfit character who gets wrapped up in a crime and the ordeal that ensues. The bizarre chain of events and, more importantly, the character's response provide a challenging philosophical trip.In "The Man Who Wasn't There", we have an identical setup. Thornton does a fantastic job portraying the stoic protagonist in a perfect film noir setting (the best justification for film noir I've ever seen) where emotions are meaningless, morals are arbitrary at best, and cause does not necessarily have effect. But what really makes this film original &amp; compelling is that Thornton, despite his seeming callousness, does not play a predictable "tough guy" but instead carries a childlike quality. In that respect, he is truly a stranger struggling to grasp the rules of society, morality and existence. He is struggling to evolve, constantly collecting knowledge, wisdom and insights throughout his adventure.At one point we realize that this may be a metaphor for the moral evolution of the human race, just as "2001: A Space Odyssey" was a metaphor for the intellectual evolution of the human race. At one point Thornton's character says he feels like he knows some great secret that allows him to rise above the din of normal society. This, I believe, implies that Thornton's character is on some philosophical plane of existence above what we know, and that is what makes this such a provocative tale.Even if you don't choose to take it on a deep philosophical level, the story is riveting, the cinematography is top notch, and the music...! Beethoven piano sonatas throughout. I'd say this film comes as close to setting the perfect mood as any film I've seen in recent years. If you have a chance to see this film, it's worthwhile on so many levels.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A Luckless Man</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Man Who Wasn't There is the Coen Brothers homage to that great novel by James M. Cain and the film made from it, The Postman Always Rings Twice. A homage mind you with a considerable influence from their Oscar winning Fargo. I loved the cinematography in black and white done deliberately I think to show the drabness in these rather ordinary people's lives. With one exception these are most ordinary folks.Billy Bob Thornton is the most ordinary of the lot. He's a barber, a guy you probably don't think too much about when you're not getting a haircut. For a barber he's a quiet sort of guy, not at all like the one he shares the barbershop with, Michael Badalucco, who's like most barbers I've ever come across, can make with a non-stop stream of small talk just to make your's and his time pass.Thornton's not talking because he's got a lot on his mind. His wife, Frances McDormand, is having an affair with her boss, James Gandolfini who is married to the heiress of the town's department store, Katherine Borwitz. McDormand does the books for the department store.Into the barbershop one day comes Jon Polito who's obviously a con man, obvious to everyone, but Thornton. Polito is trying to interest someone, anyone in some get rich quick scheme. In fact he tried to interest Gandolfini who wouldn't give him the time of day.Like Fargo's luckless Bill Macy, Thornton hatches a wild scheme to get $10,000.00 to invest with Polito by blackmailing Gandolfini and making him think it was Polito doing the blackmail. And like Fargo it ends with a few people dying before the film is over.The Man Who Wasn't There got one Oscar nomination for cinematography, I think it should have gotten some more. The Coen brothers expanded considerably on some of the themes raised in Fargo and Joel Coen as director got pluperfect performances from his cast.I said before that there was one non-ordinary character brought into this film. That would be criminal defense attorney Tony Shalhoub who's hired to defend one cast member here. For those of you who know him primarily for Monk, this is quite a different Shalhoub. Give him credit for not wanting to be typecast as Adrian Monk. Jon Polito's character is gay and he's by no means is he any kind of a good human being. He's a stereotypically gay person as would have been seen by most people in the homophobic world of 1949 when this film is set. He is one of the characters who dies in this film and the authorities don't get it right. Polito more than likely died due to homophobia back then, than for the reason they think he was killed.This is film is a worthy followup to Fargo, in some ways better than Fargo. As for how it all turns out, that's where the Coen brothers homage to The Postman Always Rings Twice comes in.</fullReview>

		<username>Critical Eye UK</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Plot That Wasn't There</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILERS AHEAD:Though responsible for a body of work that exceeds the quality of cinema wunderkind like M. Night Shyamalan (Sixth Sense), the Coen Brothers look ever more likely to be heading the same way. Nothing Shyamalan has done after Sixth Sense has been of remotely comparable stature, and whilst nothing the Coens touch could ever be as execrable as Signs or Unbreakable, the yesteryear magic of Blood Simple, Barton Fink, and Miller's Crossing is becoming an increasingly distant memory. The rot set in with Fargo, a superb piece of film-making marred by the inexplicable pretence that the movie was a true story. Neither funny nor clever, the conceit served only to destroy the context within which the movie was apprehended. With The Man Who Wasn't There, the brothers are at it again, though this time their preoccupation with context is a great deal more obsessive.The context, of course, is 1949, yet it could equally as well have been 1999: the drama that unfolds is not unique to time or place. Drama, however, seems not to have been at the forefront of the Coens' collective mind, for the meticulous narrative engineering of, say, Blood Simple is notable here only for its almost complete absence.As a result, a neat plot twist early on, via which Gandolfini's character discovers that Thornton's character is blackmailing him, is followed by nothing of similar consequence; indeed, the plot thereafter becomes so desperate that it has to reach for intervention by UFOs, an element so ludicrously irrelevant that a less self-important film maker would've had the good grace to leave it with the rest of the rubbish on the editing suite floor.Of course, it's true that the visuals are, as ever, impressive, yet the aspic of time within which the movie is locked also swallows everything else: stasis is so complete that Thornton's character is numb, dumb and troubled to begin with and numb, dumb and troubled at the end.He isn't there, as the title accurately flags up, so in the absence of a functioning human being with whom an audience can sympathise, the core of the movie is wilfully empty.And that's why The Man Who Wasn't There fails so embarrassingly: in preparing this latest offering, the Coens spent so much time on the design and quality of the wrapping that they forgot to put anything inside the box.Not worth opening: 3/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The Man Who Wasn't There: 10/10</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The opening of The Man Who Wasn't There is a simple barber pole, which is symbolic, for the main character's life that keeps on going on. The people who work in the place marked by the pole are Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thorton) and Frank (Michael Badalucco), who are brothers-in-laws. The time is 1949. Ed, our protagonist, is unhappily married to Doris (Frances McDormand). According to the frequent voice-overs, they married after knowing each other for three weeks. One day, he believes that Doris' boss, David `Big Dave' Brewster (James Gandolfini), is having an affair with Doris. So, Ed blackmails him for $10,000, not just for that, but also to pay Creighton Tolliver (Jon Polito) for helping in investing a new-fangled `dry cleaning' service. Well, things spin out of control from there. This is one of those movies where the less you learn about it, the more you'll enjoy it.My one word for The Man Who Wasn't There: wow! I'm not exactly sure how to put it into words, but it has a haunting beauty. It's a film-noir, but also a drama, thriller, and a little part comedy, and maybe a pinch of romance. Its monochromatic cinematography is vibrant, wonderful and transfixing, as is the rest of the movie. It's compelling and thoughtful, without one dull moment. I felt, well, I guess you could say I felt euphoric while I was watching this movie. Not in a happy way, but I felt one with Ed and not many movies can make me feel totally at peace with everything. It's a somber experience, but it's one I would like to take over and over.Thorton was excellent. I'm not one of his largest fans, but I felt hopelessly sad for him, and the chain-smoking barber seems to be like second nature to him. In fact, it seemed even more like a noir because everyone smoked. Just an observation. Anyway, McDormand was good, but she didn't really get the screen time she got in Fargo, which made everybody see how good of an actress she could be. Gandolfini was also excellent, and so was everyone else. Well, I thought Tony Shalhoub, as a fast-talking lawyer, wasn't as good as he could have been. But everyone else was Grade-A.The music, however scarce, was perfect for the movie, the mood, the surroundings. My one complaint? It meanders a bit in the second half, but that's not really much. Thorton's voice overs will probably make people not like this movie as much, but I thought it worked well with the movie. It wasn't the same caliber as the narration in Fight Club or Forrest Gump, but it was still predomint, and I love it. All narration, I'm a sucker for, but this was truly great narration.At the end, it got a little weird, what with UFOs and all, but reminds me of the ending of Memento with the main character realizing something. Overall, what I walked out with is the feeling that I will never look at hair in the same way again.My rating: 10/10Rated R for a scene of violence.</fullReview>

Some have criticized the slow deliberate pacing of this film noir homage, that I didn't have a problem with. I just thought the movie, while entertaining enough, added very little to the genre. Anyone who has read some James M. Cain will pretty much be able to see where this one is headed within the first half hour, but being the Coens, I expected some wildly original twist or subversion of the style, but it never came. After all, this is not exactly fresh territory for them. Their brilliant debut back in the 80s 'Blood Simple' was a modern version of classic noir, and more recently their best known movie 'Fargo' covered similar themes and situations as this one. Both those movies were more engrossing than 'The Man Who Wasn't There' for me. Billy Bob Thornton is by far the best thing about this movie. Yet another brilliant performance from him. The rest of the supporting cast are all good, especially Jon Polito, a Coen regular since 'Miller's Crossing', who plays a very shifty salesman. But while I enjoyed much about this movie, it really didn't hit the heights I was hoping for. A good movie, but not a great one, and as the Coens are capable of making such original and eclectic movies as 'Barton Fink' and 'The Big Lebowski', that makes it a bit of a disappointment for me.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Will the Coen Brothers ever make a straight movie?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Tight, funny and sad story of a self-described "barber" who blackmails and then kills his wife's lover to support a dry-cleaning business he wants to start. Beautifully photographed by Roger Deakins. The final shot which shows us that his gaze is on the very top of the heads of those who've come to see his execution is funny and revealing (as to the film's running theme of loss of identity/individuality). The writing is pretty loose though (showing more signs of brother Ethan than brother Joel), and perhaps the extraneous UFO subplot hurts more than it helps illustrate the "uncertainty principle." It's moves like this that make me wonder if the Coen Bros. will ever try to make a "straight" movie: every time they almost make it, they throw something surreal in the audience's headlights, as if afraid that their tricks will show through the medium otherwise (and I'm not knocking them, everybody's got tricks). B.B. Thornton is a joy to watch; one of his best roles yet.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Coen Film So Forgettable It Leaves No Impression - Can't remember its name!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was unable to find a theater to show The Man Who Wasn't There film in 2001 when it came out. So I had to be satisfied with watching a VHS copy of it some six months after it appeared in theaters.I should point out how fitting it is that the film has disappeared even from Video store shelves where I live. Fitting, because this is a story about the Man Who Wasn't There. For many Coen Brothers fans, The Big Lebowski and Raising Arizona are the great films. For them, the Man Who Never Was doesn't exist.This overlooked gem IS one of the great Coen Brothers films. I had to put it in my Netflix queue to get a copy. There wasn't one available locally just a year after the video came out.Lately I've been making DVD copies of favorite films. The Coens' distributors at USA films actually took the trouble to encrypt this one with Macrovision. Not that macrovision stops one. There are free programs on the net like DVD Decrypt and DVD Shrink to dispense with copying problems. It costs money to encrypt a tape or DVD, though, makes it difficult to make back your costs. Since this film is quietly disappearing anyway, distributors might just as well have skipped coding.After making the copy I sat down and watched it with the sound off and the captioning on and realized how much detail I missed four years ago when I first looked at the film. I 'read' the film this time. Billy Bob is a local barber. He is overshadowed by everyone., his wife, the local department store manager played by James Ghandofini, even by the chatterbox lead barber he works for at the shop. Crane, our hero's name, explains his story mostly in Noir voice over. Crane's wife married him because he keeps his mouth shut. The result is the two know little about one another. Man Who Wasn't There is peopled by some very forgettable folk. There's a sense of small town failure here. Everyone knows everyone in Santa Rosa, but who'd want to know any of them? A con man comes to town setting the plot in motion. Behind the small town verities, there is a Noir story dripping with deceit and personal despair. In a scene near the end, Crane is driving down a country road with a young girl. The scene reminds one of a similar scene in Cain's Postman and also of Bogey talking to the girl whose gimp leg he is trying to get fixed in Raoul Walsh's High Sierra. This film really rattles a filmophile's memory.I noticed all the references: A Place in the Sun, James M. Cain's The Postman Always Rings Twice, assorted Twilight Zone images from the Rod Serling teleplays that took place at night in suburban neighborhoods. Santa Rosa reminded me of the fictional Santa Mara of Don Siegel's Invasion of the Body Snatchers. Hitchcock and Rod Serling both put their marks on the Coens. I understand Shadow of A Doubt was also set in the bland small town of Santa Rosa, outside Sacramento. Shadows is a great metaphor. There were always waving tree shadows flitting over Billy Bob Thornton's Wan Face in the night air. Looking at the film in closed caption, I was struck by how misled I was mostly just watching the images and listening to the dialog when I first saw the film. The devil was in the details. I now know the whole story as I did not before when I took in the film, consumer style.Anyway, its a bit of a disappearing act, isn't it, this film? I keep forgetting its correct name. The Public never noticed in the first place. McDormand, Ghandofini, Billy Bob, the Coens are all part of the disappearing Man Who Never Was. Maybe that was the point.</fullReview>
	</review>

It also takes a bit of time to set up the plot, and even though it has great payoff, it's still rough to get through. Overall, it's a super solid Coen film that stands out as one of their more psychological films, as well as one of their most stylistic.</fullReview>

		<date>2015-06-03</date>
		<username>garthbarnes-11921</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Kafka: Revisited</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoilers Ahead:What is the essence of this movie: existential vacancy. It is a redoing of Kafka's THE STRANGER. Ed is not there existentially; he has gone way beyond Luke or Harry the antiheroes of the counterculture in their respective films. Ed's vacancy is so profound; like the stranger things happen to him, even life and death struggles and he experiences nothing. The profundity of the emptiness gives the film such an eerie quality. Yet, when we enter his life can we blame him for running away? How would you like to stand all day listening to Frank drone on his inanities for the entire day? When he comes home, Doris continues the droning. He is consumed with escape; he has left but his body remains. Thornton was always a great actor. Like Russell, in SOLDIER, imagine trying to act with this limited dialogue using only non verbal communication? Thornton pulls it off. I see some people thought that Ed would never have fallen for such a ridiculous scheme that Creighton sells him on.The answer is he is consumed with escape; he has retreated as far as he can, the prospect of great wealth and the final ability to leave his Stygian life. The delusion consumes him. He sets in motion the deadly concatenation of events that will ultimately kill Doris, Big Dave and himself. Thornton conveys his doubts about Creighton; I never got the feeling that he really believed him. He was so sick cutting peoples' hair and listening to Frank drone on eternally. When Doris starts an affair with Big Dave it really is the trigger. Doris is such a cold, cruel angry person that it is impossible to feel the least bit of empathy for her. Attacking the salesman, she never misses the opportunity to be mean to people. Her cruelty to Ed, flirting with Dave and subtly ridiculing Ed right in front of Dave, is why the film did not do well. It also is quite esoteric and odd even for a Coen film. When you add a heroine who is a cruel, heartless, immoral sleaze it is hard for people to care about her.It was all needed philosophically to convey the horrible life Ed has; this is why he has taken such existential flight that even when Big Dave is about to kill him, Ed still is not there. His defensive reaction is depicted as reflexive; the body saves itself automatically without any effort on Ed's part. This is quintessential Kafka. The stranger suffers these events as if he were a spectator in the stands. This is zero affective content to any action that happens to him. Even with Big Dave bleeding out and choking, Ed has no panic nor emotional content. It is the same as any other action. He simply resumes his routine. We see some of the emotional reasons Doris is cruel, cold and shut off. Yet, it is an existential flight so much further than COOL HAND Luke or DIRTY HARRY. These two antiheroes are still in the world interacting with it experiencing it. Ed is a walking corpse. He welcomes death, though, again it is surreal and someone else's experience to him. I must tell you even if all this bores the crap out of you watch the movie for Tony Shalhoub's Freddie Riedenschneider; he steals every scene he is in. His love for himself and his very flexible ethics are drop dead hilarious. Babbling anything, like the Hawthorne Effect, that he thinks will win the case for him. The entire universe has been boiled down to this: WHAT WILL WIN THE CASE? He is as empty as Ed is but for this one axion: the universe must be manipulated so I can win my case. It is a hidden gem in the Coen library far superior to the ugly, overrated FARGO. It will interest you; you will never see another film like it.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>jimbo-53-186511</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>A Cautionary Tale</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton) is a barber working at his in laws' barber shop. He's relatively unhappy with his life and suspects that his wife Doris Crane (Frances McDormand) is having an affair with her boss Big Dave Brewster (James Gandolfini). Ed gets talking to one of his customers who offers him a stake in his dry cleaning business if Ed stumps up $10,000. Ed doesn't have the money, but hatches a plan to get the money. However, Ed quickly learns that his simple and seemingly flawless plan ends up having far reaching consequences.I know that the Coen Brothers have a die hard cult following of fans, but I've never really been one of them. In fact, I've actually disliked nearly all of their films that I've seen. However, this film is different - this film really impressed me.This film is basically a Cautionary Tale - the Coen Brothers have actually made their audience consider the devastating consequences resulting in being greedy. They also make the audience consider the devastating consequences that one poorly judged action can have on many different people.The Coen Brothers have also created many characters that are not only well developed, but that we can also identify with. Ed Crane is probably the best example; for me he's an idealist, someone who wants to be recognised and remembered, someone who wants to be somebody. This becomes more apparent when he meets Birdy Abunbdas (Scarlett Johansson). Birdy becomes his project or possibly even his redemption. What I liked about Ed is that he was a flawed person rather than a bad person which meant that it was easier to relate to and care for his character. Birdy was an interesting character and it was a shame that her character wasn't developed quite as much as I hoped for - there was a lot of potential with her character which the Coen brothers didn't explore for some reason.I'll admit that in the early stages of this film that I thought the narrative was a bit shaky and found it a bit nonsensical, but as the film progressed I found myself more and more engrossed and thought that everything ended up being tied together nicely. As mentioned. it's a Cautionary Tale and is a film that will certainly make you think.The film is well-shot and the 1940's era has been captured well. Acting was roundly good across the board with Gandolfini and Coen brothers favourite McDormand arguably putting in the best performances.Overall then this is a very good film (and that's coming from someone who isn't a big Coen brothers fan). I thought that the writing was sharp, the acting was good, the plot was interesting (after a bit of a plodding first half). This is a good piece of noir and a very good film in general. Highly recommended for fans of noir or fans of the Coen brothers.</fullReview>

It is a slow-paced film noir with very dark plot twists. One could say it is a meditation not on the noir genre, but on the futility of life itself.Visually, while the technique of shooting in color and printing it in Black &amp; White does look great, Joel Coen's overall direction is really boring. There are stock characters who are disturbingly repulsive. There are seemingly pointless scenes and dialog. With the creepy foray into the Roswell incident, the Coen brothers have made yet another film that serves the interests of the CIA.The performances of Billy Bob Thornton as barber Ed Crane, Frances McDormand as his wife, Doris Crane and James Gandolfini as Big Dave inject some life. Thornton's Crane smokes like Humphrey Bogart used to and reacts to even tense situations with unruffled calm. Gandolfini is convincingly menacing. McDormand is convincing as an unfaithful drunk. Having a lawyer mention Quantum Physics to a jury (in a capital murder case), using not only Schrdinger's cat but also the Uncertainty Principle is a really absurd idea. It has to be the worst possible defense strategy, since you are telling the jury that the accused might or might not be guilty depending on when the measurement is taken.Since the taciturn Ed Crane is clearly referenced by the eponymous title, it is interesting how many men "are not there." For example, family friend Walter (Richard Jenkins) doesn't mind Ed Crane hanging around his high-school aged daughter (Scarlett Johansson, very sexy here) as she plays piano every night. I've mentioned the lawyer (Tony Shalhoub's) curious defense strategy. The two cops hardly appear sufficiently inquisitive when they are first introduced. Big Dave Brewster (Gandolfini) appears to be telling Ed Crane his secret but fails to, etc."The Man Who Wasn't There" is worth a look, but there is much better film noir elsewhere.</fullReview>

Even the whole paranoid fascination with extra-terrestrials in Roswell, New Mexico at the time the film is set (1949 - coincidentally the year I was born) emphasizes the existential dread peculiar to post-war America.Billy Bob Thornton is remarkable as Ed Crane, the anti-hero, a barber who ends up committing murder when all he wanted from life was to open a dry-cleaning store since that seemed like his manifest destiny, his 'niche' in the world. The self-effacing discontent that marks his performance makes Mitchum and Bogart seem flat and dated in comparison. There's a soft pain in his deadpan voice and brow that illuminate each chain-smoking puff as a deathwish.The dialogue is funny, astute and even beautiful. 		<shortDescription>I am a barber</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen Brothers' brilliance shines in "The Man Who Wasn't There," a 2001 neo-noir starring Billy Bob Thornton, Frances McDormand, Tony Shalhoub, Michael Badalucco, James Gandolfini, and Scarlett Johansson.Filmed meticulously in black and white, the film is about a barber in Santa Rosa in 1949, Ed Crane, a man who doesn't say much and in fact seems to be sleep-walking through life. He tries to tune out his chatty brother-in-law (Badalucco) and he and his wife Doris really don't communicate, nor do they seem to be in love. In fact, they're not.Ed may be quiet, but he seems to be always thinking. He's also observant. When he hears his wife's boss (Gandolfini) talking about an opportunity in the dry-cleaning business, he thinks he might like to do that. The buy-in is $10,000. Ed thinks he know where he can get the money. It's underhanded but worth a try.Ed's crime leads to other things, and that's all I'll say.Billy Bob Thornton, so marvelously photographed, gives an enigmatic performance as Ed. You have no idea what he's going to do. Will he blow up? What is he capable of? What is he thinking? The atmosphere created by the Coen Brothers is fraught with tension. It's like a coil waiting to spring. I doubt it's a coincidence that they chose to set their film in Santa Rosa, where Hitchcock filmed "Shadow of a Doubt." There is something very Hitchcockian about the atmosphere and the ominous feeling that danger is right around the corner.Another "don't miss this one" from Joel and Ethan Coen.</fullReview>

		<username>ElMaruecan82</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The Man Who Wasn't There, the Soul That Was With Us ...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is a cloud of absorbing poetry floating above "The Man Who Wasn't There", from the title to Roger Deakins' black-and-white photography. 'Who could be this man who wasn't there and what is 'there' referring to anyway? This is another fascinating enigma effortlessly concocted by the Coen brothers, who populated the screen with so many original characters at the edge of expressing their inner self for better or worse, and through their psychological dilemmas, inviting us to question our own lives' meanings.And it's not a surprise that the hypnotic beauty that makes this gem so worth to be discovered and watched as many times without the enjoyment being spoiled, is carried by the extraordinary performance of Billy Bob Thornton as Ed Crane, the barber. His deadpan expression all through the film provides the answer to the title, this is a man who lives his life without being quite into it? wherever he is, his soul isn't there. It's not a surprise either that he remains silent most the time, for he's the passive observer of his own life, slowly falling down in the slippery hill of routine, with quite possibly the most routinely activity ever: cutting hair. Hair have a strange habit to keep on growing, which is good for business, but doesn't leave much room for expression, it's the cycle of cutting and growth, so malicious it goes on even after death. In the barbershop, he cuts hair while trying not to get distracted by his talkative boss, and stepbrother. At home, he keeps on acquiescing to his wife (Frances McDormand), an accountant in a mall, knowing that she cheats on him with her boss, Big Dave (James Gandolfini). Yet Ed doesn't even care, and this is revealing of how totally disillusioned he is. And the detachment is not just all about attitude or a cool-looking carapace; it's the reason to be of the voice-over, carried by Thornton's virile voice and laconic tone. Because we wouldn't know what goes into that man who barely talks. The voice-over is a window on the man's soul, a man who most of the time, breathes and smokes, and only betrays his feelings with eyes-language, and it's an accomplishment for Thornton to embody the total indifference of a man toward his own failure with the only power of an irresistible sad-eyed expression. Indeed, this is the kind of bravura performance that can easily be overlooked by the Academy, more focused on larger-than-life characters but it's Crane's very averageness that makes him bloom within the darkness of his soul, to which his eyes lead like a tunnel. With his voice and look, Thornton has the stuff to rival Humphrey Bogart and Morgan Freeman, while his character is certainly no Bogart, and certainly no 'free man', still he's the perfect film-noir character. I know any film today can be labeled as film-noir as long as it features a character torn between ambitions and principles, and fatally lured into wrong choices, with a gritty atmosphere and cynical view on life to complete the picture. But the film's noir undertones go far beyond aesthetic purposes, it transcends the genre, polishes it, embellishes it, darkens it, not for the looks, but for the story. Ed Crane is a man trapped between light and shadows, in a scene where he leaves the mall after something dramatic happened, you can see his shadow penetrating a thin line of light, between two dark stains of inky obscurity, the feeling you get is his total isolation and entrapment that drove his whole life. And all his choices are motivated by the desire to get off that condition. But I might make the film sound like a sort of psychological study, there's a plot in "The Man Who Wasn't There", and a rich one, starting with Jon Polito's arrival as a con- man who'll make the kind of offer, Crane might see as a ticket to escape from his hellish routine. There are premises of great twists and revelations, but the film takes its time, and never goes faster than Thornton who is the lost soul of the film.Everyone around him talks, laughs, chats, dances, plays, everyone gives hints of their personality, or the others'. One of the most interesting characters is Riedenschneider, a lawyer, played in another stellar performance by Tony Shalhoub, the man teaches us that the more we look at people, the less likely we're to get the truth about them. And this lesson applies to Crane in two ways: the more he sees people, the more he got certitudes that end up being betrayed by reality, and the more we focus on him, the more likely we're to be surprised by his behavior. And Crane surprises us because he's a passive character on the surface but each action, each move he makes or breathe he takes, is of heavy consequences. Crane can either resign to the hypocrisy of marital wife or become his new master, make money or manage a promising piano player played by Scarlett Johannsen. This is the typical man trying to give his life a meaning, even more relevant in the late 40's since after World War II, people aspired for peace and stability, which can even be more oppressive than the exhilaration of War. Crane couldn't even go to the Army because of flat feet, and his whole life is a series of anticlimactic events, inducing a necessary appetite for excitement.The Coen brothers strike again with a noir masterpiece enriched with multiple layers of comedy, law procedural and psychological study. It's also a film of dazzling imagery thanks to its use of black-and-white and focus on relevant objects like a spinning wheel or a barber sign, working like true metaphors of boring and deadly routine. It's a nightmare around the clock turning in Crane's sad eyes, a tickling bomb that, in Crane's exceptional case, will end with an implosion.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I've watched this movie many times and while I can't put all the points together, this is just an excellent movie. This is really the only way he could contribute since the character is nearly mute in the film as compared to the three principal people in his life: his wife, Doris, played by Francis McDormand; his brother-in-law and lead barber at the shop, Frank, played by Michael Badalucco; and his wife's boss, Big Dave Brewster, played by James Gandolfini. They don't seem to care either way and neither does he.While surrounded by blabber-mouths, Ed stoically carries on, cutting hair and just occasionally wondering about the meaning in the universe (his consideration at one point to mix cut hair with "common house dirt" is startling for this character as his brother-in-law shows). But Ed soldiers on and on, taking some sojourns along the way but really not leaving the path life has led him to all along.It's such an interesting character and such an interesting way to present him. The Beethoven piano sonatas which account for, I believe, the complete soundtrack, is perfect for Ed's on-screen musings and short forays. Like Ed they are considered, solemn, quiet, and in the end, stunning.This has to be Thorton's best role by a mile (well, maybe half-a-mile remembering "Slingblade"). Of course, the movie is filled with many familiar Coen Brothers' faces who are all excellent and carry their chores with grace. The writing is tight and the film flows along like a Beethoven sonata. If you're looking for "Fargo" or "Big Lebowski", this is the other kind of film the Coen Brothers make, beautiful and stunning ... nearly enough to leave you with your mouth hanging open by the end.A film not to be missed.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Movie for Fans of the Coen Brothers</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Reactions to this interesting black-and-white film noir are likely to depend on whether you like the Coen brothers' sensibility or you don't. It will certainly not be to everyone's taste. The principal character, well played by Billy Bob Thornton, is a taciturn barber in a small California town. He's intentionally a nobody in a nobody setting who gets caught up in a spider's web of interlocking crimes. I won't say anything about the plot, which is a variation on the scenarios of other noir films. It's strength lies in the odd, often humorous perspective that the Coen brothers bring to all their films. This one is different than some of their previous work because the humor is, for the most part, subtler and the principal characters are intended to be ordinary people living ordinary, uninteresting lives in a mostly uninteresting way. But the acting is excellent, drawing on the talents of actors like Frances McDormand who are regulars in Coen films, newcomers to their stable (James Galdofini) and the young Scarlette Johanssen. My wife and I are Coen devotees, so we liked it. Others may find it slow-moving before it finally gets going. There's no one in the movie to like, not even Johanssen in the end. It's in black and white, which many film-goers don't care for. And film noir is film noir -- a passion for some, not so for others. See it or don't accordingly.</fullReview>

As his character Thorton does not show emotions via his expressions because he communicates them through the few words he does speak. 		<username>Stuart Richards (The_Eighth_Passenger)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>The Coen Bros. film nobody ever talks about</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Man Who Wasn't There revolves around and is narrated by a man named Ed Crane, a barber, under-played brilliantly by Billy Bob Thornton. Displeased with his profession and seeing an opportunity to buy into a dry cleaning franchise he decides to blackmail a local department store manager (James Gandolfini in one of those great 'character actor' parts he specialises in when he's not on TV) who is having an affair with his wife (Frances McDormand). Naturally, nothing goes to plan and one disaster leads to another as people are killed, betrayed and falsely accused. The best way to describe the film is as "typically Coenesque" and while that is a huge compliment it is also what works against the film. There is a definite feeling of been-there-done-that that lingers over the whole thing but I suppose that even the Coens cannot break new ground with every effort.If Miller's Crossing was about "men in hats" (Barry Sonnenfeld), then The Man Who Wasn't There is about shadows. Many of the most memorable shots in the Coen catalogue occur in this picture and it's obvious that Roger Deakins is truly in his element. I would venture to say that visually The Man Who Wasn't There is the greatest film from the brothers, although No Country For Old Men is also a contender for that title.As you'd expect, the performances are great across the board. The Coens excel in casting the right actors in the right parts. Even though they frequently use the same performers, it feels fresh every time and not like they are just casting their mates 'cause it's easier. Along with the aforementioned, Jon Polito and Tony Shalhoub chew up the scenery in great supporting parts and it's a nice surprise to see a young Scarlett Johansson pop up and display her natural talents early in her career. Read into that what you will...The Man Who Wasn't There is certainly worth your time and it fits nicely into the Coen canon. It is by no means their 'worst' film (that will always be Intolerable Cruelty, which is still decent) but just don't go into it expecting anything overly original. Indeed, besides its black and white photography it doesn't really have much to separate it from their other work, that is to say that amongst their filmography you will find similar subjects being discussed in better films. Criminal plans gone wrong? Check. Disillusioned central character? Check. Frances McDormand having an affair? Check. You get the idea. Still, The Coens prove that even when they are treading water they can deliver a better product than most directors going for gold, which is something they should be pretty proud of!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Neo Noir</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen Brothers pay homage to Film Noir by essentially making the film as it may have been made in 1949, the year of its setting. It is a comedy but a dark one, with a low-key approach that stresses the absurdities of life. Thornton is well cast as a barber who thinks he has found a means to escape the daily rut but instead finds himself involved in murder. The fine cast includes the likes of Gandolfini, Johansson, and Shalhoub as interesting characters. The cinematography strikingly evokes the atmosphere of a bygone era that is nostalgic as well as tinged with darkness. Beethoven's "Pathetique" Sonata is effectively used to convey a mood of melancholy.</fullReview>

		<username>friwan</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The man who wasn't there - A modern classic!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This has got to be one of the unsung gems within the coen brothers' collection of classics. With an entourage of films such as Fargo, Barton Fink, The Big Lebowski and Oh Brother Where Art Thou, (it is very ironic to say that) it's easy to overlook 'The man who wasn't there'.Starring, a middle aged Ed Crane (Billy-Bob Thornton) working as an assistant Barber in his brother-in-laws barbershop in 1940's America, narrating and sharing the events of his life in a retrospective manner. You are introduced to his calm, quiet character somewhat content with his mundane life, his job, his cheating wife and his house. Nothing in his life is great but also nothing seems to be wrong, everything is OK.Following through his daily routine, and his humdrum existence he continues on day after day cutting hair until one day a particularly optimistic customer walks through the door. A salesman has come into town to present his new invention to a local business to arrange a financial partnership for the $10,000 funding required to start, his 'Dry Cleaning' company. His idea is duly rejected and he is left looking for someone else for the capital.Though clearly not having the money Ed decides to step in to 'raise' the amount required through means of blackmailing his wife's adulterer. Hereon the story of the quiet simplistic man develops, twists and turns through the result of obtaining the money.Shot beautifully in black and white film noir style, the camera frames and angles used in some of the scenes are unique and wonderfully placed, but yet simplistic. In a real Coen Brothers style the film never really takes a particular path and as soon as you feel it becomes predictable another story seems to develop, forever changing the outline of the film.Billy-Bob is exceptional in his role and with the Coen directing his screen presence is immense, even despite the fact that his character is based on someone who has primarily in his life taken a back seat. With the support of the impressive Tony Shalhoub as his fast-talking lawyer and Frances McDormand as his wife and even a minor part for a younger Scarlet Johansen.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>strange and worth seeing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is one of the strangest films I have recently seen and it's not much of a surprise considering it's brought to us by the Coen Brothers--famous for some of the quirkiest plots in history. Like Raising Arizona, The Hudsucker Proxy and O Brother, Where Art Thou?, this is a weird but ultimately enjoyable film with lots of crazy twists and turns. While I appreciate this type of film, I should point out that some Coen Brother films take turns that are just too weird or fall flat--the prime example being Barton Fink. I should also say that MANY viewers simply won't like or appreciate the strange twists, so I would think reviews for any of these movies would be extreme (either you hate the films or you love 'em).Billy Bob Thornton plays the lead like he is a bit of a zombie, but I assume that is how he was supposed to play it. He's a barber married to a wife who is cheating on him with her boss. Billy Bob hears about a scheme to make money but needs startup money, so he decides to blackmail his wife's lover by sending an anonymous threatening note--pay $10,000 or we'll expose your affair. All this happens in the just the first 1/4 of the film. Where the plot goes next and the MANY odd little diversions I'll leave for you the viewer to find out BUT I will say that there are many plot twists, quirky performances and a subplot involving aliens--all placed in a black and white photographed world that looks like it's from an old Film Noir flick. Perhaps, at times, there are a few too many twists and the movie could have easily eliminated a few of these subplots, but this is a minor quibble.A final note: If you watch the Coen Brothers' film INTOLERABLE CRUELTY, you'll see Billy Bob do a character who is almost 180 degrees opposite the one he plays here. I really think this was a great inside joke and is worth a look!</fullReview>

		<date>2006-01-03</date>
		<username>paul2001sw-1 (paul2001sw@yahoo.co.uk)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Beguiling but artificial</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Joel Coen's films are all comedies, but they tend to divide into two groups; riotous farces ('Raising Arizona', 'The Big Lebowski'), for which I don't particularly care, and stylised, deadpan movies such as 'Fargo' or this film, which I personally prefer. This latter class of films are in fact so deadpan you might not even realise they are comic, although viewed straight, they might appear to be a little heartless. 'The Man Who Wasn't There' tells the story of a hapless, unemotional barber who lives in a small town in 1950s America: he hits upon a scheme to make money, but everything goes wrong. The initial premise of the scheme is this: he discovers his wife is having an affair, so he elects to anonymously blackmail his cuckolder under threat of exposure. This idea is typical of the film as a whole: preposterously dispassionate, but presented without any hint of explicit smirk. Them movie is shot elegantly in black and white, echoing the films made at the time of its setting, and Billy Bob Thornton is great as its almost silent "hero"; it's a strangely beguiling story, in spite of taking place in a wholly constructed universe that is an deliberate mixture of clich and utter originality. Some similarities to the work of Jim Jarmusch can perhaps be detected here, although in my mind, there's a little less soul, and a little more show-off cleverness, in Coen's work (even if the latter is completely absent from the superficial tone). Ultimately, this movie doesn't really have much to say about the human condition; but it's wonderfully assembled and executed nonetheless. In conclusion, it's a distinctive oddity, albeit one that is very much aware that this is what it is; and one of Coen's better movies.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>When in Santa Rosa...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>We're being asked to think about Hitchcock, who used this quintessential small town for "Shadow of a Doubt." But what makes Hitch's movies disturbing is their insistence on the reality of absolute evil -- you can never dismiss anything in them as a fantasy or a breakdown in reality, like the burning hotel in "Barton Fink" or the last-minute rescue of Norville Barnes from certain death in "Hudsucker Proxy." The Coen Brothers' movies, while hugely enjoyable, are a little too jokey. This is a dark story, but its tone is too mixed to find a place in one's nightmares; the UFO story is ludicrous, the reference to Heisenberg totally unnecessary. After a while, you just count the references to other films. (Tolliver at the bottom of the lake, his hairpiece drifting in the current, reminded me of Shelley Winters in "Night of the Hunter.") But Thornton is perfect.</fullReview>

His wife is working at a department store and sleeps with her boss. Suddenly an offer falls into Cranes lap. If he can just get a hold of 10.000 dollars this is the opportunity of his life. But how? Many people would say that the greatest work of the Coen brothers now lie a while back into the past. I would have to agree with that although i have seen nothing wrong with their later movies. The thing that i can say though is that they have perhaps become more and more about a polished surface than a rich inside so to speak. And this movie sure does not lack in visuals. Filmed in glorious black and white, every crease in Billy-Bob Thorntons face is showing like canyons in a landscape. The murder story is noir standard-fare you might say. And of course that is somewhat of a problem. You are not very likely to be surprised when knowing the genre lets you see where this is going early on. Still it's a fine display of film-making. And even if it won't have you holding your breath to the end it is still a rewarding experience. If the Coen brothers would have made it less a show of stylish film-making, and more of a gut experience, this could have been something truly great. Now it's just very good. I rate it 7/10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>what is 'modern man'?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When freddy reudenschneider asks this question, the cat is out of the bag--'the man who wasnt there' is being held up to us movie-goers as the quintessential example (via Coen Brothers) of what the post-world war II life would be like---a bungalow in the suburbs, a dull job, a listless and loveless marriage, a cheating spouse...and here we are, not just in santa rosa, CA, but all over the western hemisphere, replicating this experience.This movie is being sold short by viewers as simply an artistic acheivement when it ought to be explored, thoroughly in fact, for the authentic presentation of the American adult experience that it portrays. I agree with many of the other reviewers that it is well-made and contains intersting performances, but viewing in its historical context is where this movie succeeds. Living in an age where 'box office success' is most often used as an indicator of what makes a film 'great' or 'memorable' allows only a few chances in a year to see stuff that really matters. "The Man...' is one of those movies, not seen by anywhere near enough people...</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Style and Substance in Equilibrium</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is the kind of movie many people today call "slow". Other guys, like me for example, come out of the cinema thinking "not all is lost, thank heavens", walking right into the next lamppost. The Coen Brothers and their team got style. But style never rules over substance in this one.They really succeded in emulating the high quality fotography of the forties and early fifties that characterizes the classical Film Noir. (Just look at the scene in the supermarket with the pianos!) But that's not all. There is always - beyond the references, the props or even the general plot - a message or a philosophical comment lurking in the background without revealing itself openly. It leaves lasting imprints in the memory.I share the Coen's apparent fondness for phonies. Basically Ed Crane is a lone, innocent, naive human surrounded by them - and no flying saucer will carry him away. "Coen stock actors" like McDormand, Polito and Shalhoub are as effective as well casted Gandolfini and Scarlett Johansson (looking like Teresa Wright in Hitchcock's Shadow of Doubt). Richard Jenkins as the morose small town lawyer has some great moments, too.The monologs and dialogs are splendid. The mention of famous "Schroedinger's Cat" is well placed and nicely embedded in one of expensive defense-lawyer Riedenschneider's ramblings. The most striking exchange was for me the one between Ed Crane and the piano teacher/talent scout. Crane just does not understand why his protge will most probably not become a world famous pianiste, why the might of sheer willpower is put in question by a small fat foreigner. I don't know the right term for this kind of conversation in which arguments just don't get through, I just know: That's life. All over the world.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>This film got under my skin. Each is very different, and in this movie, Billy Bob Thornton gives an amazingly restrained performance where we almost will our own feelings into the character. This is a man who has moved through his life with an expression that suggests he knows what's going on or is one step ahead. 		<username>rzajac</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Moral revisitation of Fargo, then some...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is an entrancing film to watch, like a masterful ballet of light and word. This film should be watched if only to bask in the film work.I notice many people comment on the artful use of B&amp;W. I think it's interesting that they shot in color and then more or less relegated the film to a tech shop for B&amp;W post-processing. I'm not accusing the emperor of having no clothes; I'm just saying it's fascinating that a film shop was able to render such a visually fine final product.I think of "Big" Dave's line: "I'm not proud of what I did. But you...". That's *the* line in the movie, and harkens back to Fargo; a line Jerry Lundegaard could have easily said to his father-in-law--if not for his standard-issue Minnesotan reticence.Ed and Doris Crane are at one and the same time clear messages and ciphers. What do they want? Why did they really get together? That Ed appears to be both repressed *and* free--a contradiction in terms, to be sure--is a stunning achievement.This movie is a black hole, sucking in every bit of light you may have shed on it in hopes of getting insight bounced back. Is it a fair portrayal of life without God, set in the post-War era? Or is it really just about sex? Should we apply Augustine, Freud, or Jung to the task of making sense of the Cranes, and the cast of characters knocked about in the moral event storm whirling about their heads?I think the Coens are always trying to find ways to make us confront the second-hand algorithms we use to process moral questions. These methods may work 98% of the time, but life throws you the occasional curve ball, and I think movies like "The Man..." inoculate us against these eventualities by giving us cinematic representations of that remaining 2%.Oh. And did I mention the saucer people?</fullReview>

And, like "Fargo", the snowball effect takes his world by storm. Will he be able to get a grip on himself? The Coens have an unique capability of achieving something that looks paradoxal - every film they make stands out from the others, making their films so different between them. And "The Man Who Wasn't There" is probably the crowning jewel of their chameleon-like cinema: i'm not even talking about the magnificent black and white cinematography, i'm talking about the way the hero is portrayed, the way things happen and the way they are solved, all this greeted by a way of filming and editing that pays hommage to film noir, but ends up being much more than that. 		<shortDescription>If the people in Nighthawks Could Talk and Move Around</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>People who are uncomfortable in an art museum probably won't take to this movie. It doesn't offer the quirky characters of previous Coen legends and it's not set in our lifetime with familiar sets and props such as car dealerships and bowling alleys. Instead, The Man Who Wasn't There draws mesmerizing sketches of archetypes from a past era--sketches we only know from black and white photographs and black and white films. This movie plays on our familiar conception of the past as black and white. The characters are whittled down to a few motivational impulses, perfectly suited to the contrived old-Hollywood plot. Everything about this film is exquisitely crafted, from the chiarascuro of each frame to the precise dialogue to the brilliantly engineered plot.I'm guessing that those who have the gift of appreciation for paintings and photographs will love this movie.</fullReview>

		<username>Alain English</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Good premise spoiled by over-long, meandering plot</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>With a premise that is both simple and interesting, but spoiled by an overlong plot, "The Man Who Wasn't There" fails to deliver the kind of depth and pathos it requires to make an impact on its audience.Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton) is a quiet, unambitious barber living in a passionless marriage to his wife Doris (Frances MacDormand). Wherever he goes he is a largely inconspicuous prescence barely noticed by anyone. He ambles on regardless, not appearing to care. He then suspects his wife of having an affair with the chubby, cheerful businessman next door Big Dave (James Gandolfini) and decides to make a change to his dreary existence by making a deal with a shady businessman (Jon Polito) to blackmail Big Dave for money to start a dry-cleaning business...The plot is well-written and possesses alot of strong elements. But unfortunately the pace drags considerably during the film's second half. The result is that alot of scenes feel largely extraneous, neither contributing very much to the plot or deepening the characters. The black-and-white look of the film, whilst never entirely pleasing, does contribute to the sense of inertia felt by the characters, especially Ed.With a reputation for taking on unique challenges as an actor, Thornton gives a strong but flawed performance as Ed. He is good at conveying the boredom of the man and his aimless existence but it is a largely humourless performance in a character that is never really given any depth. We are never told why Ed has such an apathetic attidude towards life. Thornton dominates the film to such an extent that many of the supporting players (comprised of some popular actors such as James Gandolfini and Tony Shalhoub) are rendered largely forgettable.A brave effort by all concerned to do something a little bit different, yet the film tries too hard to be "deep" and "heavy" and the lack of levity and humour make it's potentially rich premise very unpalatable.Disappointing.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>It has been a long time since I loved a Coen brothers movie. Gone are the days of Raising Arizona and Blood Simple. Somewhere along the way something warm seeped away from the films. Everything since Fargo, and including Fargo for my money, has more seemed an advert for how clever the filmakers are this time, instead of how compelling the motives and drives of the human beings within the film.This latest film, The Man Who Wasn't There, for me walks into the same shallow water as Lebowski, Brother, and Fargo, the water of slick style over substance and meaning, of self-congratulatory cleverness. No doubt about it, the Coens are doing their homework, because intellectually it all hangs together rather nicely, enough to please most critics at least.I loved the black and white camera work in this, and I can't say anything against the writing. It was tight. The acting was seamless, although I find Frances McDormand always seems the same in a Coen film: no surprises.For me it was a soul-less exercise in smarts, lacking blood. When I remember Miller's Crossing I recall emotions, mine engaged with the characters'. When I think of Barton Fink I remember the feelings I had identifying with the characters. Blood Simple gave the hairs on the back of my neck a tickle. Raising Arizona tickled me all over from the belly out.What I got from The Man Who Wasn't There was a big so what? Who cares? A nice looking bit of wooden characterization moving in a fog through a polished gem of a story that says nothing about love, fear, hope, or much of anything, except film itself.Their movies used to be about more than just movies. Nowadays they're just chokin' the story-making chicken.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Did the Coens peak with Fargo? Judging by this, absolutely not. Returning to the bleak, surreal comedy-drama that distinguished earlier works such as Barton Fink and Blood Simple, the Brothers Weird have knocked out a beautifully-shot, deeply resonant modern noir. Billy Bob Thornton turns in a quietly magnetic central performance as Ed Crane, the man who just "cuts the hair", a barber in forties small-town California with a motormouth wife and a face that would make Clint Eastwod say "Damn, he's craggy". When a dry-cleaning pimping travelling salesman drops by, a series of events begin to unfold that push Ed into a life-altering decision. Detailing the various plot twists and character turns is a meaningless excercise, as without the beautiful cinematography and the subtle nuances of the supporting cast the story means little. See this film, and marvel at it's fantastically-warped take on life, love and dry-cleaning.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Genius stuff</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>SPOILERS ALL OVER ITEd Crane is a laconic barber who discovers that his wife is cheating on him and, indifferent to the fact, decides to take advantage from it by blackmailing her lover to get money to invest on dry cleaning. His plan goes wrong when the blackmailed man asks him for help and then, discovering that he planned it (or maybe not, it's not at all revealed)attacks him as he screams: "What kind of a man are you?". Crane kills him with a knife.This killing scene is absolutely wonderful. Crane is driving his drunken wife to bed and he begins to remember the day he knew her. Suddenly the phone rings. It's Great Dave, the blackmailed guy, asking him to go to his office (he's already paid). Crane goes there and finds a devastated man. Great Dave punches him and begins to strangle him. Crane kills him and walks out slowly of the building. He goes back home, sits in the same position where he was when the phone rang, and continues the trail of memories with the same sentence he was using before.The movie's full with those little, subtle and clever details that really make a Coen film. Let me say something about the Coens: they never do what you expect. Their movies are either excellent or very good (with the possible exception of "The hudsucker proxy"). But they NEVER do what you expect from them. Not only in the storylines, absolutely fresh, original and twisted as hell (during the course of "The man who wasn't there" not once did I guess what was about to happen), but also on their philmography. After filming a version of the Odyssey set in the 1930's in America and reminiscent of the Preston Sturges comedies ("Oh Brother, where art thou?") and a hillarious modern interpretation of "The big sleep" that replaces Phillip Marlowe by The Dude, a former hippie who lives trying to make the less amount of effort possible ("The big Lebowski") they come up with this. Genius stuff.Besides this the movie has other atractives. The acting is absolutely wonderful, almost unbelieveable. We'd already seen how great Frances McDormand was in other Coen movies. Plus we got Billy Bob Thornton (who BECOMES Ed Crane), Jon Polito and Michael Badalucco. And a special mention for Tony Shualhob (or however it's spelled), the star of the great TV series "Monk": this is one hell of an actor, believe me. And the black and white photography is superb. I know there's a color version of this movie shown in the theathers in Europe, and I simply can't imagine it. This movie should be seen in black and white.I don't recommend this movie to everyone, though. Joel and Ethan Coen're not everybody's cup of tea. Someone might complain about the very (deliberately) vague script, the very personal sense of humor or might get bored by the slow, contemplative way in wich they shoot. But if you wanna get your chances, go for this picture.SAY NO MORE</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>anglowelsh</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>quality.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The b+w may not be perfect, but this film has the Q word all over it. Thornton is quite magnificent, as are just about all the other actors. The atmosphere is spot on, and the plot convincing enough to make you both sad and - perversely - happy (for Crane, for his relief) at the end.If you've seen the Coens' 'Fargo' (or, for that matter, Falling Down) you'll instantly recognise the theme of a man's world falling apart despite his best intentions to manipulate it into doing what he wants it to do. For Crane it's all about escaping - a mind crushing job, a marriage of misery, a town full of people content to comply and ignore anyone who refuses to do the same - familiar enough feelings for most of us. Whether he can escape or not depends, however, not only on his strength of character but on circumstances that never fail to lead him to disappointment. But from the start, everything is against him, and he helps it all along by submitting to some flag-wavingly dodgy schemes (Polito, as another superbly grotesque Coen character) and a major moment of albeit desperate action that could possibly seal his fate.The atmosphere and a couple of sequences imbue a strong element of sci-fi, (nothing is ever straightforward for the Coens) including a scene that appears to pay homage to Close Encounters. This is not as incongruous as it sounds, as Crane has basically been plunged into the Twilight Zone, one in which he has become a living ghost. Being invisible has its advantages of course, but the lagubrious Crane routinely fails to take them. The final scene sees justice finally dealt, and Crane's hope for something better undiminished.</fullReview>

		<username>AP-Project</username>

		<shortDescription>Atmospheric, Subtle Period Piece</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I loved this movie. I love this time period in American history when people dressed in nice suits, wore hats and had good manners. It was also a time when the appearance of impropriety was something a person would avoid at all costs. A person's public reputation was quite important. Against this backdrop, the Coen brothers have concocted a story of blackmail and internecine deceit that eventually envelops all the principals. I love the story and the "quiet desperation" of Ed Crane's character is something many people try to keep hidden while going about the tasks in their mundane lives. While Ed doesn't say much in the barbershop, his mind is attuned to his circumstances and he knows that he's not quite satisfied with his identity; that there should be something more out there for him. The story really works for me and I found this much more satisfying than Fargo, which had an obvious (and unsympathetic) villain, and overly prolific use of those Minnesota accents. Thornton is terrific in this movie. He has the patience and calm to play this quietly intelligent man, and I don't think many other actors could handle it properly. I'm not crazy about the very end of the movie, but I believe the Coens added that to say that crimes are pinned on people simply to "solve" them, which makes the public feel safer and that was certainly a major priority in that era (as it is today). I highly recommend this film. It rates in my top five (of any genre) from the last 2 years. This is a great movie. I give it a 9.5 out of 10.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-07-29</date>
		<username>A.J. LaFollette</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The Coen brothers are a national treasure.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Somehow, even now in the benighted 21st Century, Joel and Ethan Coen still continue to schnooker American studio bigwigs into paying them good money to make interesting, unique films. All I can say to them is, "Thank you"."The Man who Wasn't There" takes its sweet time to build up tension, and never quite reaches a conventional climax. The plot meanders through various left turns, random scenes that don't fit into the narrative, and scenes that seem to take place merely to allow the Coens and cinematographer Roger Deakins to play around with light and smoke and enjoy how cool it all looks on B&amp;W film. No matter. The whole thing is a load of fun. Unlike the overly slapsticky "O Brother", this movie is played completely deadpan, which requires the viewer to pay close attention in order to catch the underlying comedy, but the payoff is worth the effort.The pacing is, as many here have noted, quite slow and deliberate, but the Coens have a wonderful sense of the absurd that keeps the movie from falling into boredom or melancholy. While the conservative, conformist dullness of post-war America would seem to be an overly easy target for satire, the Coens manage to avoid most of the easy choices. The true villain here isn't greed, or heavy-handed government, or social conservatism, or any of the usual targets of film noir... although all of these evils make an appearance. Rather, the villains of this movie are the lives of quiet desperation that ordinary people lead after they sell out their dreams in exchange for security. Ed Crane's life falls apart after his schemes backfire... but he is somehow curiously redeemed as a human being. Billy Bob Thornton is simply perfect in the lead role, displaying an amazing range of emotions without ever cracking a smile or raising his voice. Tony Shalhoub chews up scenery as a celebrity defense lawyer. Frances McDormand is stellar as always... I suspect Joel Coen would cast her in his movies even if she wasn't married to him. It's simply a top-notch film... and even in the places where it takes a wrong turn, it still manages to be worth watching.</fullReview>
	</review>
