		<date>2002-06-19</date>
		<username>DahManDatWuzzinDare</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Coen Brothers' clever homage to classic film noir is probably their finest film to date.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>CONTAINS SPOILERS!! While it may an overlong, gushing homage to the classic film noir and Hitchcockian detective mysteries the Coen Brothers grew up on, The Man Who Wasn't There has the same quirky sense of humor that made such past Coen brothers films, like Barton Fink and Fargo the acclaimed masterpieces they are today. Though it is slightly inconsistent and the well-constructed screenplay is thrown off towards the end (there are several points towards the third quarter where the movie could end and the plot would still be cohesive) it is still their finest film. The exquisite black and white photography of Roger Deakins is on a par with Vilmos Zsigmond's work in Altman's The Long Goodbye. The moving shots add a sense of poignancy to each movement and emotion. The sophisticated direction of Joel Coen adds a naturalistic sense to the shots. The Coen Brothers have always worn their influences on their sleeve, but never as much as they do here. The Man Who Wasn't There might be their most serious film, even though it's still a comedy, and it's a welcome change of pace.The film is set in 1940s suburbia and perfectly depicts the desolation and depressing lives of those within its confines. James Gandolfini delivers his finest film performance as Big Dave Brewster, a department store owner who employs hard-drinking Doris Crane (Frances McDormand.) Doris's husband, Ed (Billy Bob Thorton) is a seemingly mundane Barber, who suspects her to be having an affair with Brewster. In the opening scenes we are introduced to his world, as boring as it may be. The Coens fill the scenes with strange characters and images as Thorton describes endless details of the various popular hairstyles of the era. This helps up get inside the mind of Ed. He senses the events going on around him, but the rest of the world sees him as a dolt. "The barber," as he puts it.One day Ed is cutting hair and overhears a drifter (the hilarious Jon Polito) named Tolliver talking about an incredible new enterprise called dry cleaning. A way of cleaning clothing and linen with chemicals without the problem of shrinking clothes or leaking colors. He asks Ed if he would be interested in funding the stores. Ed takes it into consideration and decides to blackmail Brewster for $10,000 (the amount Tolliver needs to get the store started.) Brewster's store is owned by his wife's family, so if word got out that he was cheating on his wife, he'd lose everything, so he pays up. Ed has Dave drop the money in the garbage can of the hotel where he plans to meet Tolliver. He gets the $10,000, gives it to Tolliver and goes off on his way.At the same time Ed meets Birdy (Scarlett Johansson.) The daughter of his friend Walter (Richard Jenkins) and becomes fascinated by her nack of playing music. Their relationship is another interesting sub-plot of the film. Ed believes she has a gift and begins seeing her and watching her play piano as the saga with Doris and Dave unfolds. One night Dave calls him over to the store to tell him that he knows it was Ed. After an altercation, Ed stabs and kills Dave in self-defense and makes off clean, but Doris is charged with the murder. Attorney Freddy Riedenschneider (Tony Shaloub) is brought in to defend her. The day after Ed privately admits to them that he killed Dave, Doris kills herself. Subsequently Ed is informed that she was pregnant with Dave's baby at the time. He never seeds Tolliver or his blackmail money again.Fed up with the bad choices he's made Ed decides to take Birdy for piano lessons that he would pay for. Upon meeting the instructor (Adam Alexi Malle) he learns that Birdy has no talent. On their way back Birdy wants to make it up to him and attempts to perform a sexual favor for him in the car. Through the confusion he crashes the car. This is the point where the film gets a little cryptic. Ed flashbacks to his "happy" life with Doris before she died, sitting around silently in their suburban palace thinking of something to say to each other. When he comes to he is charged with the murder of Tolliver (who was actually killed by Big Dave Brewster after the money pick-up.) His story is too complicated to believe so he is charged with Tolliver's murder and sentenced to death.While the end is a little cryptic, and the "magazine interview" angle makes for an abrupt ending (be it a drawn out one,) The Man Who Wasn't There is the best film of the year. It's dark, funny and morose. All the things that make the Coen Brothers films great. It is also intelligent, witty and emotionally charged, which is what makes it a great film. While the Coen brothers have made a few clunkers (the Hudsucker Proxy, O Brother Where Are thou) they have yet to make a film that isn't a laugh per minute or a shocked expression per minute. It's hard to say that this is a great film noir, because every nuance of the film is borrowed, but the characters are likeable and interesting. It isn't a film but a moving portrait of restrained emotions and hopeless lives.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2002-06-11</date>
		<username>WriConsult</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Pure Coens, with a hidden "agenda" (**SPOILERS**)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>OK, so I'm a Coen fan. These guys can take a genre and reduce it to its purest form in a single finely-crafted movie. "The Man Who Wasn't There" definitely follows the pattern, with its sense of foreboding, plodding pace, painstaking filming and editing, and more Noir "hooks" than you can shake a lit cigarette at. It's absolutely beautiful to watch, and a loving homage to a genre that is referenced more often than it is experienced these days. And while I'm not a big Billy Bob fan, his performance in this role was impeccable and riveting.Yet for all the craftsmanship, its pace was slow and it seemed a little empty, like something was missing. The missing element is found in the answer to the movie's central question: "What kind of man are you?"*** SPOILER FOLLOWS ***So what kind of man is he? A gay man. Yes, I believe Billy Bob's character is a closeted homosexual. Think about it. He hasn't slept with his wife in years, and doesn't seem too bothered by her ongoing affair: his interest in it is primarily in how he can use it for blackmail. The salesman tries to make a move on him in his hotel room, and the situation is made all the more embarrassing by just how SURE the salesman was that he'd be into it. And of course most upstanding citizens would be distressed to have their friend's teenage daughter making sexual advances on them, but the way Billy Bob reacts to Birdy is different than you would expect out of most other men.He lives the very safe, straight barber life because given his orientation, and the straightjacket of the times, it is very important that he appear that way. And why stick his neck out and be adventurous, when society won't allow him to be what he really wants to be anyway? As far as society's expectations were concerned, he truly Wasn't There.One of the wonderful things about Noir is the way in which it provides a photo-negative mirror image of the times in which it was made. Paradoxically, its very darkness illuminates the unacknowledged anxieties and misfits overshadowed by happy, optimistic glow of the times. Yet despite Noir's insights into the postwar world's dark underbelly, some things were off-limits even to this genre. "The Man Who Wasn't There" hits on an issue that, while topical today, was too deep and dark even for Noir at the time. The Coen brothers have not only distilled this form down and perfected its essence, they have extended it. A great cinematic achievement.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Not bad, but not as good as others have said</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Maybe I'm just impatient, but I found the first half of "The Man Who Wasn't There," pretty boring to sit through. Admittedly, sometimes there are things you don't notice until you've seen a film multiple times, so maybe I missed some of those the first time through, but it seemed like the first half could have been shortened considerably. The second half of the film makes up for the first. There are quite a few surprise plot twists and director Joel Coen builds a lot of tension and creates a sense of dread.Billy Bob Thornton is great as the main character. He says so much even when he seems to be doing nothing at all. The rest of the cast is also impressive, especially McDormand and Shalhoub. Also impressive is the cinematography. Roger Deakins does so much with black and white that one could easily mistake it for one of the older film noir pictures that obviously influenced the Coens.What I'm less than thrilled with is the script. In addition to the first half of the movie dragging, there are a couple other problems. I'm not sure what Scarlett Johansson's character was in the movie. Don't get me wrong, she did a good job, but I don't see the importance of the character. Again, perhaps I'm missing something. Also, a lot of the dialogue seems to be self-consciously trying to be "noir," that it becomes cliche and annoying. Lines like "Me, I just cut the hair," would be funny if the delivery wasn't so serious.Overall, "The Man Who Wasn't There," is an enjoyable half-a-movie. The first half drags, but the second half is very good film noir drama. It's well acted despite the flaws in the script. It's worth a look if you like this kind of thing.Rating: 6.5/10</fullReview>

		<username>Edgar L. Davis (luludavis@aol.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A thoroughly entertaining and artful film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen Brothers are one of the few 'complete' filmmaking teams in the film industry and this could not be more evident than in the film, "The Man Who Wasn't There". By 'complete' I am referring to the attention that is paid to every aspect of a film. Director Joel Coen frames every shot as if he has studied the paintings of Edward Hopper(an important that made art during the period of American history that this film is set in). The sharp contrast of light and shadow coupled with continous views through opened doorways and windows gives the film an almost three dimensional look. The use of the classical piano Beethoven piece is featured in the opening credits and is introduced throughout the film at key moments until it becomes part of the characters movement and thoughts. And who else but the Coen Brothers can make a character's tragic descent seems simultaneously comical and (with the closing lines uttered by Billy Bob Thornton) spiritually uplifting. And let's forget that Frances McDormand is the most versatile actress in film today. She is incredibly sexy, dangerous and vulnerable as Doris Crane, the wife of Billy Bob's character.I've never hated, lusted after and then felt deep compassion for a film character as I did for Mrs. Crane. Other great performances were given by such underrated actors as Tony Shaloub, Michael Badalucca and James Gandolfini.</fullReview>

		<username>FawltyFan87</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A film noir in the classic tradition with a fresh perspective.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I must admit: I am not a big fan of the Coen brothers. I thought Fargo was a meaningless gore-fest that left you in the middle of nowhere (pun intended). But for some odd reason I LOVED The Man Who Wasn't There! I suppose the only explanation for this is that the film noir genre is one of my favorites, and believe me: The Man Who Wasn't There lives up to every precedent that has been set by such films as Chinatown and The Maltese Falcon. Yet it still maintains that touch of class, that fresh look that keeps it up-to-date, despite the fact that it is set in the twenties/thirties. I understand why some people didn't like it, though: it certainly has a sort of run-on feel that may bother some (and intrigue others). But that's how many classic movies are! In my humble opinion, the sole purpose of The Man Who Wasn't There was to tell a good story and never break a sweat. And when you look at it from that perspective, I believe you, too, will find a new respect for it. First of all, Billy Bob Thornton is amazing (as always): he plays the part of the lonely, solemn barber to perfection, yet his acting is so subtle that it doesn't distract from the rest of the film. It is only in retrospect that you can go, "wow: he really was incredible!" Secondly, the music is beautiful: the piano solos and string harmonies are quite reminescent of Fargo (which was the single good aspect of that film) and they fit in perfectly with the overall morbid mood. And, of course, the cinematography is simply astounding: the black-and-white coloring was necessary to give it a true film noir feel, but the lack of color doesn't even subtract from the overall beauty of the movie. In fact, it adds to it! As you watch the events of the story unfold, you are completely immerged in the film, experiencing every emotion that the lead character undergoes. And the ending is especially tragic, leaving the viewer with something to think about and perhaps even a tear streaming down his cheek. All in all, The Man Who Wasn't There is a crowd-pleasing nostalgia that is not easily forgotten.Overall rating: 10/10</fullReview>

		<username>(bsmith5552@rogers.com)</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Film Noir is Alive and Well!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Man Who Wasn't There" represents the Coen Brothers entrance into the dark shadowy world of Film Noir. Shot in black and white by cinematographer Roger Deakins, this film takes us back to the classic film noir features of the 40s and 50s to the days before widescreen and living color. The story set in the late 40s, centers on "second chair" barber Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton) who works for his brother-in-law Frank (Michael Badalucco) and who's life has evolved into a loveless hum-drum existence. His wife Doris (Frances McDormand) is having an affair with her boss "Big Dave" Brewster (James Gandolfini) who is married to the heir of a chain of department stores (Katherine Borowitz). Along comes business entrepreneur Creighton Tolliver (Jon Polito) who offers Crane a chance to escape his drab lifestyle by investing in his dry cleaning venture. Naturally Ed doesn't have the $10,000 required and decides to blackmail his wife's lover. This is when the story develops more twists and turns than a pretzel factory. Thorton, who is rapidly becoming one of the Hollywood's best actors, is outstanding as Ed Crane. He plays him with an air of mystery, a sort of a time bomb waiting to explode. McDormand is excellent as the unfaithful wife, Polito suitably oily as Tolliver and Gandolfini in an all too brief role, shines as the brutish "Big Dave". The scene where Ann Nirdlinger tells Crane about a close encounter with aliens is a bit baffling and unnecessary to my mind. Tony Shaloub does a nice turn as lawyer Freddy Riedenschneider (Try saying that three times in succession). The big star of the piece though is the amazing black and white cinematography of Deakins. One is fortunate these days to see ANY film shot in black and white, much less a masterpiece such as this. The contrasts, the lighting and the shadows are all there and shows that film noir is alive and well and living in the 21st century.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Another score for the Coens!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Man Who Wasn't There" is a beautiful film all-around. Visually, it's spectacular. And you can't ask for a much better cast. It was interesting to hear the way the Coens perceived this movie. Listening to the audio commentary, I noticed they perceived "The Man Who Wasn't There" as a dark comedy. My initial impression of the movie was more tragic than funny. And I think I'll still perceive it as a tragic story, especially since your sympathy for Ed Crane grows as the story continues. But the story is also intriguing, and the film does borrow elements of an old-fashioned film noir. I recommend this movie to anyone who is a Coen Brothers fan, or anyone who is a fan of good films in general. As I said, it's an incredibly beautiful film!My score: 8 (out of 10)</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Fantastic ... if you're in the mood</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I rented this on Saturday and watched it with my boyfriend ... who was so bored he put me off it and I had to try again when he was out!! I loved it, he hated it. It's that sort of movie.Visually, "The Man Who ..." is beautifully shot in atmospheric black and white. This movie is in fact so beautiful that it's easy to be distracted from the story while admiring the cinematography. At times it feels like some plot points are being drawn out for too long because the Coen brothers have a fantastic idea for some visuals and they are determined to show it off to us.The plot starts out as classic film noir and is well summarised in other comments here. The addition of a flying saucer (briefly) doesn't hurt it. In fact, I thought that more than anything this movie was about dislocation, that Ed was helplessly outside his own life, unable to control any of the things that happen around him (even when he tries to exert some control, by taking Birdy to a piano teacher, it all goes horribly wrong), because he really isn't "there" in any meaningful way. It's as if he made a choice to separate himself from human interaction: for example he is virtually silent in the barbershop, and at one point he says he has not had sex with his wife in many years. Despite his wish to be all but invisible Life comes up and knocks on his door, and the only decision he ever makes (to obtain $10,000 by blackmail) is the catalyst for everything that follows. Guess you can hide from the world, but it won't hide from you.I loved the way black and white worked with Billy Bob's craggy features and enjoyed the slow pace of this movie. If it had been more action packed and had a slicker plot, I wouldn't have spent any time thinking about his character, which is possibly the Coen's intention.Either that, or they sit at home and read all these reviews and laugh their pants off!See this movie, but make sure you're prepared to relax into its gentle flow. I give it 8/10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>neo-noir at its finest</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I love the Coen brothers. This film is another one to tack up to the wall of success for these guys. The Man Who Wasn't There is neo-noir at its finest. Ofcourse it is not a carbon copy of the 40's Genre. The reason this movie should be rented by any film enthusiast is because it combines great direction, acting and plot. When a director can accomplish this it is rare (not so much with these two brothers) and must be seen.The reason this movie is somewhat different from other noir films is because there is no Femme-Fatale: The woman to coerces the man into doing evil things for her. This movie uses Billy Bob perfectly as a mono-tone barber who creates a web of intuige and uncertainty with one gut decision. I won't go any further, but you get the picture. It is also filmed in black and white to extentuate the underlying shadyness in this picture. Another film technique that makes this film a joy is the use of a voice over narration from the main character. From start to finish Billy Bob narrates his inner-monologue so the audience can get a taste of the mind behind the character. Rent this movie the next time you go to the video store, you'll enjoy it's cleverness and unique qualities.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2002-05-21</date>
		<username>jhclues</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Yet Another Amazing Performance by Billy Bob Thornton</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In 1984, brothers Joel and Ethan Coen established themselves as filmmakers with their debut film, `Blood Simple,' a noirish thriller that heralded a new and unique voice among the denizens of the cinematic world. Since then, the Coen Brothers have crafted and delivered a number of memorable films, featuring eclectic storylines and diverse characters, all of which have something to say about who we are and what makes us tick; all of the things that make the world go ?round. Whatever the subject matter, at their core their films are all examinations of the human condition; character studies that illuminate the infinitesimal possibilities wrought by that thing we know as human nature. Whether it's through the ruminations of the Dude in `The Big Lebowski,' or the musings of a pomade wearing escaped convict in `O Brother Where Art Thou?,' the Coen's films are insightful, as well as entertaining, and always unique. When you walk into a Coen Brothers film, you never know what you're going to get, but you can bet it's going to be an experience. And `The Man Who Wasn't There,' directed by Joel Coen, and starring Billy Bob Thornton and Frances McDormand, is no exception. In it, they revisit the `noir' territory of their first film, while contemplating the suggestion that there are things in life that just may be predestined; fate may provide a choice of paths to traverse, but in the end, they all seemingly lead to the same place. It's 1949, in a quiet town in California; a place that suits Ed Crane (Thornton) just fine. Quiet and unassuming, Ed is the second-chair barber in a small shop owned by his brother-in-law and first-chair barber, Frank (Michael Badalucco). Unlike most barbers, Ed doesn't like to talk. He just likes to cut the hair. Which works out fine, because Frank talks enough for them both. Ed suspects that his wife, Doris (McDormand), among other things, likes to drink a bit too much; he also suspects that she may be having an affair with her boss, department store owner `Big Dave' Brewster (James Gandolfini). But Ed's not one to get too worked up. Over anything. In his quiet and unassuming way, he's an observer; a watcher of the life being lived around him. Which pretty much suits him just fine. Almost. Ed goes along, cutting hair and watching; sometimes wondering. About his wife; about life; about what it means to just blend in. And then something happens that momentarily shakes him loose of his cocoon: A salesman, Creighton Tolliver (Jon Polito) blows into town with nothing to sell but an idea; but it's an idea for a revolutionary new concept that will make him-- along with the right investor-- rich. And Ed is interested. Because all he'll have to do is invest, let Tolliver do the work, and he can just...blend in. It's the perfect plan, but like all perfect plans there's a flaw, beginning with the fact that Ed doesn't have the money to invest. But through a lifetime of watching, Ed has learned a thing or two; and now, he has a plan of his own. A plan that once put into play will trigger a chain of convoluted events beyond anything he could ever have possibly imagined. And before it's over, Ed will understand what the guy meant when he said something about `best laid plans.' Because his best laid plans are about to change his life forever. And not necessarily for the good. With an approach that is every bit as quiet and unassuming as their protagonist, producer Ethan and director Joel deliver their most subtle film yet. The story (which the Coen's also wrote themselves) perfectly captures the era in which it is set, and is fraught with the unexpected. The Coen's weave a web of twists and turns that are almost nonchalantly intricate, and rarely take you where you think you're going. As in real life, things happen-- the import of which is often not realized until after the fact, and then, only after the next event-- prompted by the first-- occurs. And as the film has a somewhat mesmerizing quality to it-- aided in no small part by the brilliant B&amp;W cinematography by Roger Deakens-- it is later, upon reflection and after having had a chance to thoroughly digest the events you've witnessed, that you will feel the real impact of it all. And it's then, most probably in a quiet and unassuming way, that you will say to yourself, `Wow--' The Coen Brothers are gifted storytellers and filmmakers, they know what they want to say, and moreover, know exactly how to get it across to their audience. All of what the Coens bring to the table makes for a good movie, but in this case, the single element that takes it beyond `good' to `truly inspired,' is the performance by Billy Bob Thornton as Ed. In one performance after another, in film after film-- beginning with his dynamic portrayal of Karl in `Sling Blade'-- Thornton has veritably created a category of actor unique unto himself, that of Leading Man/Character Actor. Like no other actor, he has the ability to truly transform himself with every new role, so thoroughly that not a trace of himself remains. And each character he creates is totally new, physically as well as psychologically; it's as if Thornton is the product of some kind of artistic schizophrenic existence that enables him to become just about anyone he chooses. It's a rare talent, and as he demonstrates-- again-- with his portrayal here of Ed, it's a talent he uses wisely, and most effectively. And it makes you wonder if there is, in fact, a REAL Billy Bob Thornton. He is truly, one amazing actor. The supporting cast includes Scarlett Johansson (Birdy), Richard Jenkins (Walter), Katherine Borowitz (Ann) and Tony Shalhoub (Riedenschneider). Thought provoking and transporting, `The Man Who Wasn't There,' reaffirms the unique genius that `is' The Coen Brothers. I rate this one 8/10.     </fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2002-05-20</date>
		<username>john zapata (johnpaulzpt@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the Coen brothers' best</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen Brothers' new movie, The Man Who Wasn't There, is a film noir shot it glorious black and white. In the tradition of their masterpiece Fargo, this movie also depicts a simple plan that just goes totally wrong. It all starts with a plan to obtain money, and ends up with murder, unexpected twists, and unraveling tension. Although The Man Who Wasn't There does not go as deep as Fargo does, I still very much enjoyed its well-written story and excellent cinematography. The title is very appropriate to Billy Bob Thornton's Ed Crane, a barber who seems aloof to the world around him. `I don't talk much; I just cut the hair,' he claims, and its true, all he does is stare and smoke his cigarette. He is married to Doris (Frances McDormand), and he knows she is having an affair with her boss, Big Dave (James Gandolfini). Unfortunately, Ed Crane couldn't care less about the affair. When a customer in the barbershop proposes a business deal for $10,000 (for a new invention-dry cleaning), Ed decides to blackmail Dave. He sends him a blackmail letter, asking for $10,000 or else the affair will be revealed to Dave's wife, Ed himself, and everybody else. Because he is a respected member of the community and wants to gain control of his wife's business, Dave has no choice. This is good news for Ed. Except there's one problem: Dave knows about the man with the dry cleaning idea.Using black and white cinematography and simple camera angles, the Coen brothers have produced simplistic shots that are reminiscent of old movies. I don't know if the movie will also look good if shot in color, but I enjoyed it the way it is. I also have no idea why it was shot in black in white. Is it because of one sequence where a man bleeds to death, or was it because it is a film noir? Nonetheless, I'm sure the director had his reasons, and I did enjoy the black and white atmosphere.Billy Bob Thornton shows development as a very versatile actor. I also admired his performance in Monster's Ball as a racist prison guard who falls in love with a black woman. He displays his versatility by playing a bank robber in a comedy Bandits, which although I have not seen, the few trailer scenes probably tell it all. But I think his portrayal of Ed Crane is his best performance of the year. He manages to look the same and aloof all the time, even in times when great emotion is needed. This performance fit the character perfectly; he convincingly portrays the man who wasn't there.As I mentioned earlier, The Man Who Wasn't There does not go as deep as Fargo does in terms of the characters and the insight to the human condition. But The Man Who Wasn't There is perfect in its own little way. I was captivated by its story development all the way through. The screenplay is so well written, complete with smart and sometimes fully dialogue. After the dreadful Oh, Brother Where Art Though last year, the Coen brothers bounces back big with The Man Who Wasn't There. This is one of the best films of the year.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Casey Machula</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A man is nothing more than his reflection in the mirror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There is a certain illness one feels whenever part of an organization (or society) that doesn't see you for who you are, does not care about who you are, and only wants to see an image, stylized to fit one of a few socially acceptable `types,' instead of the reality. We've all encountered it, at work, at play, at school, even in our neighborhoods. `Let us prepare a face to meet the faces we meet' is the great line from `The Lovesong of J Alfred Prufrock' (TS Eliot). The Man Who Wasn't There is about a man who is invisible in society: when he's seen, he's reduced to a `type': a husband, a barber, a taxpayer, a relative ? anything but a human with drives, internal direction, spontaneity, love, or a soul. His life is reduced to set of carefully circumscribed roles. And, lacking any proclivity to self-examination, he never wonders why the collective reflecting pool is shallow, so dark, and so empty. He accepts the necessity of preparing a face to meet the faces he meets (he is, after all, a barber, so this is his profession) as a matter of course; and, when he cuts hair, he does so as an automaton, lacking the gritty sense of self that would allow him to follow his own inspiration. He wonders at hair, for example, that it grows as a part of one's self, and is just cut off and discarded. The obvious question he needs to ask himself is, `How many parts of myself have I cut off and discarded?' The thought never occurs to him. Instead, he seeks to service his image in the traditional American way: by getting rich. Billy Bob Thornton narrates this story, this prosaic human tragedy, as if in a coma of detachment from himself, reminding us of those other lines from Prufrock, `Let us go then you and I, While the evening is spread out against the sky, Like a patient etherized upon a table.' See this movie, if you dare.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-04-28</date>
		<username>Cinema Buff</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Ah, the Coens...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Joel and Ethan Coen are the Hollywood equivalent of that guy you know who tells you the most blatant lies with a straight face. He's probably joking, but...you never know.They have made some of the finest, most well-constructed, and oddest films of the 80's and 90's. And through it all, they have fooled us left and right. They claim that "Fargo" is based on a true story. It is not. They claim that "O Brother Where Art Thou?" is based on "The Odyssey". They've never read "The Odyssey". They claim that their editor is the Oscar-nominated Roderick Jaynes, whom they have used for numerous films. They actually do all of their own editing, and Jaynes is a pseudonym they use.Perhaps the most distinguished characteristic of the Coen films is their fascination with borrowing from a genre without completely imitating it. "Miller's Crossing" was a mob movie where the Coens made up their own slang. "The Hudsucker Proxy" was a slicked-up Preston Sturges screwball comedy. And now, with their latest, "The Man Who Wasn't There", the Coens have tackled 40's film noir.The story centers around barber Ed Crane, played to subtle comic perfection by Billy Bob Thornton, who gets an unusual customer one day. A man with a plan. "Drycleaning. That's right. Washing...without water!" Crane is intrigued and wants in on the plan. All he has to do is raise $10,000.There is much, much more that happens, but I dare not reveal any of the details. What follows is a smorgasbord of double crossings, murders, flying saucers, rambling philosophical monologues, and of course, haircuts. And through it all is Crane, nodding slightly, blowing an endless stream of cigarette smoke from him as if repel all that come near.Thornton's performance deserves immense recognition. In a typical film from the Coens, overacting is abundant. Indeed, the supporting cast (of whom the most notable are Frances McDormand, Tony Shalhoub, Michael Badalucco and Jon Polito) are firing on all cylinders, delivering every line like it may be their last. Thornton, however, sees his world crumbling around him, and he barely flinches. His Ed Crane is a natural poker player, one who shows nobody what he is feeling.Like most Coen films, it is a visual treat. Roger Deakins' black-and-white cinematography shines with the nostalgia of the Golden Age of Hollywood. Various shots, such as a car flying through the air in slow motion and a pair of doors opening to reveal a brightly lit death chamber, stick in your mind long after the theater lights come up. Carter Burwell's score is haunting, as with most Burwell scores, and it blends beautifully with the sonatas of Mozart and Beethoven.And as expected with the brothers, the script is a marvel. The Coens are never one to take a cheap shot, and they always manage to leave you at a point where you have no idea where the film will go. The script zings with dialogue that perfectly represents the paranoid, post World War II era. And the plot contains small tricks to make its audience smile (Crane, during one of his voiceovers, is interrupted by a phone call. After about 10 minutes of surprising plot twists, he is back at his house, and the voiceover continues).This film ranks amongst the Coens best. It is not as original as "Fargo", as intriguing as "Blood Simple", or as funny as "Raising Arizona", but it is a large success on all scales that make the Coens two of the most beloved filmmakers in modern Hollywood.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>It's about miscommunication, and by the look of many comments here, they failed to communicate that theme.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I have a real love/hate relationship with the Cohen's work. I think "Raising Arizona" and "Fargo" are outstanding yet I loathe "Big Lebowski" and "O'Brother". This great film falls into the former. Under it's black and white/film noir veneer, lies a story about communication and miscommunication. As a people we are more prone to mistaking what is meant than comprehending. We live in a world of underlying thoughts, past experiences, implied agendas, and non-verbal messages that cloud over the communciation process. We are left with only frail words to attempt to convey our meaning. That is why Thornton's character wants to avoid "gabby" people, because he wants to avoid the futility of trying to communicate (and the realization that most people talk on and on without saying much). The final scenes bring the point home as we witness he and his wife helplessly trying to talk on their couch and in his last words where he hopes to see her in another world where there are things other than words to make his point.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-04-23</date>
		<username>Drew (andrew7@erols.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Noir, with just a hint of comedy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton) is a very quiet man. He's one of those characters who creates awkward silences in almost every scene, as he fails to play into the standard conversational banalities that everyone else does. Ask him a question, he answers it with as few words as he can. You have the information you asked for, and he fully expects you to be satisfied with that. He doesn't talk about himself much (even as the narrator, he tells you what happened to him, but doesn't tall you directly much about himself at all). This is not a man who shares, or opens up, or anything like that.I wonder if a character like this is easier or harder to play than a more extroverted character. I imagine it would be harder, and it's definitely harder to make a character like this likable. When a character keeps all of his emotions inside and only allows you to see his actions, which are also meticulously guarded and controlled, it's hard to find him sympathetic. As a result, even when an actor rises to this considerable challenge, as Thornton does in this film, he is often overlooked by moviegoers who consider the performance lacking (e.g., "He didn't do anything!"). Other strong performances underrated for similar reasons are Brad Pitt's in "Meet Joe Black" and Tom Cruise's in "Eyes Wide Shut".Just look at Thornton's face. It's remarkable. Especially if you've seen at least a few of his other films. You can look at Ed Crane and think "Oh yeah, that's Thornton," but upon reflection, Ed Crane doesn't look much like Thornton's other characters at all. That alone is remarkable. Compare to Kevin Spacey, for instance, who is another excellent actor.... most of his most famous characters look (and often talk) exactly the same. Thornton creates wildly dissimilar characters, even with a script that makes him work overtime to create a character at all.Not that I'm complaining about the script. The script is masterful. As is the direction and (especially) the cinematography. Yes, it's very slow. I know. If you don't like movies that take their time to delve a little deeper into the characters and story, well... skip this one. But if you're willing to be patient, you're going to see a hell of a story, with some terrific plot-twists, and several remarkable performances. Frances McDormand is rapidly stringing together an impressive array of exceptional performances, just in the last few years. She's had exquisite supporting roles in this film, in Wonder Boys, and in Almost Famous. And that's ignoring her previous work in films like pre-Fargo films like Blood Simple, where she was no less accomplished, but a lot less recognized.If you're a Coen brothers fan, I think you'll like this. I am, and I did, so... why not? But this is a different sort of Coen film than I've seen before (as most of them are). There is humor in this film, but it is very low key, very understated, and very beside the point. The humor works to alleviate the tension at key moments (usually at moments when most films would be upping the tension, which is a nice change of pace). The humor also exists to alienate Ed Crane from everyone else. Ed doesn't seem to respond to humor.I've only seen this movie once, and I should like to see it again soon. The one thing that doesn't really sit well with me is some of the things that fly in from left field, like the UFOs, the Heisenberg uncertainty principle, and so on. They certainly have the effect of introducing some recognizable Coen absurdities to the proceedings, but they seem to undercut the loving homage to the noir era that the film tries so hard to get across. I'm not sure what the intention was with that stuff, but it didn't damage my enjoyment of the film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Superbly done faux film noir in black and white.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Edit: Watched it again November 2015, I still find it superb, one of the more interesting movies I've seen. Thornton in a performance of a lifetime.SPOILERS - Something about the trailers for "The Man Who Wasn't There" made me want to see it as soon as it came out on DVD. So I rented it yesterday upon first release. I enjoyed the first half of the movie a lot more than the second half. The B&amp;W filming gives it a great atmosphere, and BB Thornton plays Ed, the barber of few words, just perfectly. In fact, BB "is" this movie. I believe he will be remembered as one of the fine actors of our time. Many popular actors, like Travolta, Denzel, and Hanks, are really just different faces of themselves, there is a sameness about all their performances. But not Billy Bob. Take 4 films, for example -- this one, "Sling Blade", "Pushing Tin", and "A Simple Plan." All different roles, almost different people playing them, but all BB. But I digress.This film is a tribute, and a spoof of, the 1940s "film noir" genre. Set after the WWII, in 1949, Ed goes through life almost as a "man who wasn't there." He sees an opportunity to invest in the leading edge of new technology, "dry cleaning", but has to blackmail his wife's boss and lover to get the $10,000. Boss finds out, confronts him, is beating Ed, when a quick jab into the neck severs the artery. Ed's wife, who had cooked the books for her boss, gets arrested for the murder. Wife hangs herself in prison, but later Ed gets accused of the dry cleaning investor's murder, he gets an incompetent lawyer, who plea-bargains "guilty" and Ed goes to the electric chair.The story is not so important. Anything could have happened to still make the point of the film. Ed was a man who wasn't really there, even in his own life, and the big things that could have gotten him, didn't, but he met his demise for something he didn't do. That's the irony, the spoof.The film is most enjoyable for the masterful cinematography and the sound editing. Regardless of where the story was going, I was just caught up in the atmosphere of this film. The DVD sound and picture are crisp. But the DVD itself is rather disappointing. The extras are not particularly interesting, and they are not entertaining. Especially the commentary track which does nothing to address the filmmakers' intent during key scenes. They don't seem to identify at all with what we viewers might be curious about. Instead they talk here and there about what they found interesting.Still, for someone who likes somewhat offbeat films, and this genre, it is a good movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

And no, they don't. There is only the string of jokes, the self-consciousness of the Coen style, and a simple human life ending in a thud. Most telling weakness: the movie cannot decide whether its main character was a stone-faced seer or hopelessly dense, and neither can we.</fullReview>

		<date>2002-03-15</date>
		<username>willy calloway (bedazzle11@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>interesting theoretically, lacking in presentation</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Spoilers, interpretation:Presentation: Overall, I like the theoretical aspects of this movie ? although there are certain problems ? it's mostly the presentation that made this less than a must-see movie for me. The pace is incredibly slow to start and only picks up momentum later when it actually gives you something to think about. Yes the black and white is moderately interesting and there are some creative screenshots, but it's just not enough to keep me interested. The humor was also a different brand from that which I enjoyed in ?The Big Lebowski' and ?Fargo.' Mainly two types of humor are used: expectation and randomness humor. A line is uttered and you can already foretell the response and you're supposed to laugh because you're smart enough to see this. The problem with this type of humor is that for the audience to expect the next line the joke itself has to be cliched. An excellent example of how trivial this humor is is the fact that everyone laughed when the line from the trailer came up, `they put on their pants one leg at a time just like everyone else.' The Coen's did not mean for this to be funny, but it illustrates the point. When this gets old, random humor is used ? the humor of unexpectation. For example, when Ed says he'd like to collect the hair and mix it with soil and the spaceships and the sudden attempt at fellatio. We later learn that these occurrences were not actually random, but they are randomness humor nonetheless. Personally I prefer less slapsticky humor such as irony and creative social analysis and such.Theoretically: So Ed is the modern man? What does that mean? Ed is a barber. He is like Sisyphus who is condemned to eternally push a boulder up a hill. Ed must forever cut hair that just keeps growing back and even grows a bit after the body is dead. So spiritually is hinted at. Ed is not a completely a modern man because he admits the possibility of a future life both here symbolically and in the end directly.Besides the agnosticism, Ed is more optimistic than the typical stereotypical modern man. He is bored with life but doesn't pass into renunciation; he tries to create meaning, or at least entertainment. Specifically, this occurs when Ed gets suckered into the dry cleaning scam and when he tries to get something by living through the piano player. So with Ed's lack of words, his boredom is symbolized. Also, his lack of language shows his impotence in dealing with his position as modern man. Yes, Ed narrates the movie and clearly has an excellent ability to describe, but he cannot get at his essence. This is shown when Ed cannot defend his faith in the piano player's talents. He knows that he feels passionate about the music, but cannot explain why, just like he can't explain why everything turned out the way it did.Another anti-modern-man theme is the movie's poetic justice. Sure, Ed is convicted in an absurd way that clashes with the reality of what happened, but in the end he is justly punished for a crime he has committed. He could arguably be considered innocent of murder because of the self-defense defense, but he is clearly guilty of criminal negligence in this instance and of blackmail earlier. A more accurate portrayal of chaos would have done without the poetic justice since that requires some sort of metaphysical foundation which is clearly not acceptable here. The spaceships and Ed's wife's reappearance in the end are used to get us past these qualms, but they seem contrived and unconvincing.An underlying problem with this movie is the misrepresentation of the Heisenberg uncertainty principal. It doesn't state that the closer you look at something the less you know about it, it states that when taking measurements of subatomic particles it is impossible to determine both the position and momentum of the particle at the same moment. This is far from the metaphor of chaos it is used as. An accurate representation would say that when looking closely at something you can only know part of it. And that is only at one time; it is possible to know more if given more observations. You can never know everything because the eye is limited to perceiving one thing at a time. So we have order, just no absolute order, at least from what we know. The idea of chaos could still be used, it should just be explained through the pseudo-philosophy of some character (probably the lawyer again), instead of being used under the guise of actual science.The ending was slightly redeeming and made the lackluster beginning and middle worth sitting through, but it's definitely not my favorite Coen brothers film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>great film if you can stick with it</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In this stylish, mesmerizing, black-and-white film noir, Billy Bob Thornton plays Ed Crane, a nearly comatose barber in post-war Santa Rosa. Thornton's rugged features capture and reflect light like an Ansel Adams landscape, giving him remarkable screen presence even as he remains nearly motionless except to sweep the floor or drag off an ever-present cigarette. Frances McDormand holds up her end as his wife, and a variety of other larger-than-life players add life and laughs as they dodge into and out of a slowly twisting plot.Crane hopes to break out of a deep rut by sinking some money into a mysterious new technology called `dry cleaning.' In the wondrously twisted universe of the Coen Brothers, dry cleaning is described in terms that make it seem slightly more fantastical than the flying saucers that appear sporadically in this film. This is part of a major theme -- that our perceptions of reality are, in many ways, flawed and confused. In a similar vein, the movie is rife with characters misinterpreting the actions, intentions, and motivations of their friends, lovers, and acquaintances. And when folks inevitably start dying, both prosecutors and defense attorneys show little interest in knowing or considering facts that don't help to build their respective cases. The upshot is that, despite the serious flaws inherent both in our perceptions of reality and in the workings of our justice system, capital punishment has long enjoyed the support of the American people.</fullReview>

		<username>Aidan McGuinness</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Well acted, pretty, and mostly enjoyable fare</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Man Who Wasn't There" (or TMWWT) is an amusing film noir piece from the Coen brothers. It centres around Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton), a quiet barber who decides to raise some cash, to invest in a business opportunity, by blackmailing Big Dave (James Gandolfini) because Dave is having an affair with Ed's wife (Frances McDormand). From there it all goes downhill...The highlight of TMWWT is that it looks gorgeous. The Oscar nomination for cinematography is extremely well deserved. It's shot in high quality, crisp black &amp; white. The shots are all well framed, and the lightning all done with a great degree of technical precision. In fact it looks almost too good for film noir - there's none of the gritty, real feel of most film noir movies. Joel Coen is adept here, giving us long lingering shots of Crane staring off into space, a cigarette drooping from his lips, and yet able to capture the more fantastical elements - that creep in later on - with equal ease.The acting too is worthy of note. I felt Frances McDormand didn't really draw me in as much as she has done before (most noticeably in the Coens' "Fargo"), but the rest of the cast were great. Gandolfini, as usual, was throwing his muscle and presence around but Thornton deserves particular notice. He delivers a dry, not-quite-happy-or-sad performance as Ed Crane, and seems to let the events of the movie wash over him with a remarkable nonchalance.The film's biggest flaw is the Coens' script. It seems to meander along, and Ed Crane just seems to get carried with it. He isn't so much reacting to one event after the other, as just being pushed along without a bother. There's obviously a certain amount of humour being injected into the proceedings - particularly as the plot grows more and more absurd and thus there's a problem as to what the film is trying to achieve - it can't quite be a typical film noir homage, and yet it certainly isn't a comedy either. This somewhat uneven pacing left me with a vague feeling of dis-satisfaction at the end, as to what exactly I had just seen.I certainly enjoyed "The Man Who Wasn't There" and, as it is in IMDB, I'd place it in my Top 250 movies... but it wouldn't get into the Top 50. There's something lacking from it that has it being good, but not great. 7.5/10</fullReview>
	</review>

I wouldn't mind seeing Joel Coen win an Oscar or at least a nomination (has he ever been nominated?) for his direction. All their movies are worth watching and so is this one. Just sit back, relax, and enjoy.</fullReview>

As with all good thrillers of this style, the humour, unforced, is much funnier than in films which try to be comedies. 		<shortDescription>Genius!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Yet again, the Coen's prove that they are the most original filmmakers working today, with a twisted story of murder, extortion, and hairdressing. Ed Crane, played with remarkable ennui by stone-faced Thornton, is a barber caught on the hop when he tries to blackmail his wife's lover for $10,000, in order to invest in dry cleaning. From then on there is accidental murder, suicide, and eventually some kind of justice. Crane narrates the full story with such a deadpan delivery, and Thornton performance is so spot on that you find it difficult to either like, or loathe, the character. But it seems to me that that's the point. He really 'isn't there'. Other characters hardly react to him, and when he confesses to the murder it is ignored as being implausible. Because the character is so anonymous, and yet needs to carry the film, the supporting parts are peppered with some of the best lines, and performances. Frances McDormand, as Crane's wife, and Tony Shalhoub, as the flashy lawyer stand out, and counterpoint Crane's nonchalance.This is a fantastic movie from every point. It looks perfect, with sublime lighting and use of black and white, that really enhances the story, and the script is full of the usual off-kilter humour and one-liners that mark the Coen's work. That they can still produce a movie of such originality after previous commercial success is genius!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Film Noir Revisited</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen brothers are more popular in Europe than in the States, which is not strange since they constantly choose to go in the opposite direction of the regular Hollywood strategies. These genius brothers dare to be different and they do it superbly time after time. The Man Who Wasn't There is no exception. Film noir is a more or less dead movie category today, and this film serves as a modern tribute to that gendre. Thornton plays the discontent and misplaced barber who tries to make things better for himself, only resulting in making things worse. The characters in the film are as in any Coen movie exceptionally interesting, especially the smaller roles. If you look past the humor, the flawless style and the sense of watching an updated movie with that great 40's feeling, you will notice that the film is made with more heart and deeper meaning than any other Coen movie. I am one of the few who think that Fargo (1996) is not one of the best Coen films. In my opinion their best work include Miller's Crossing (1990), The Big Lebowski (1998) and O Brother, Where Art Thou? (2000). I can confidently add The Man Who Wasn't There to that impressive list. Rating: 8/10</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2001-12-08</date>
		<username>fred-287</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Sometimes less is less</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Whenever I see a movie by the Coen brothers, I pay them the compliment of trying to forget who they are and just watch the movie. It's very hard not to like these guys. Clearly they love movies, have seen most of them and lavish doting detail upon their own. They write clever scripts and find a way to let each actor shine in his or her part, however small. Sometimes they even have some pungent points to make about life. And yet.... There was only one Coen flick I found really gripping: their first, BLOOD SIMPLE. Shot on location in Texas on a budget of next to nothing, with a cast of unknowns (of whom only three--Frances McDormand, M. Emmet Walsh &amp; Dan Hedaya--were ever heard from agan) and a script that on paper must've read like the cheesiest Z-grade potboiler, they made magic. I was immersed deeper than a born-again Baptist. The Hedaya burial scene had my heart pounding; when it was over I felt as drained as the guy doing the burying. The final sequence had me on the edge of my seat, where I never found myself again until, I think, SEVEN. I was so immersed that even the arty stuff, like the bullet holes through the door each producing a shaft of eerie light, or the newspaper flying towards us in slow motion while McDormand and the guy talked in regular motion, or the foot-level dolly shot, etc.--all that not only didn't bump me out of the action but enhanced it for me. I left the theater in a kind of daze. "Who the hell are these guys??" .....But after that it was as though they'd contracted terminal self-awareness. Haven't seen all their movies since then but quite a few, have found something to like (sometimes a lot to like) in all of them, but each time it's like I'm watching myself watching it. They won't let me lose myself in it. In a way they're the opposite of David Lynch, whose stuff is disturbing because he's so good at drawing you in and then leaving you trapped in that unsettling dream logic. The Coens are disturbing because they leave you standing outside the fence, almost daring you to climb over it. So as I said at the outset, I try to forget these guys are probably geniuses who could do whatever they wanted, and just take what they give me. On those terms, I'd say my favorite from them has been FARGO, which came the closest to BLOOD SIMPLE in letting me care about what I was watching, as though the characters were actual people about whom I could read in the paper--in fact, wasn't that the one the Coens originally claimed was based on a true story?--and my least favorite has been BARTON FINK, but again, I haven't seen them all. Special mention for the ashes scene in THE BIG LEBOWSKI where I almost fell out of my chair laughing, I appreciate a movie that can make me do that. ....So here we are with THE MAN WHO WASN'T THERE. Well as always with the Coens, there's a lot to like. Billy Bob Thornton is aces with me for a number of reasons (but he'd better do right by Angelina, goddammit) but chiefly the way he stays rigidly within each character's emotional range. His "big moments" are those of the character, not the actor. Think how rare that is, think how most of the time Joe or Jane Star will turn on the waterworks or otherwise "play up" a big scene. Not Thornton, and it's probably cost him several Oscars, especially for SLINGBLADE &amp; A SIMPLE PLAN. Mrs. Coen (I forget which one is her husband) again can do no wrong. The period detail's impeccable, although just once in my moviegoing life I'd like to see one where the vintage cars have some dents &amp; grime on them, don't look like they're from someone's vintage car collection. Some nice black-&amp;-white compositions, like the silhouette shots or Gandolfini sitting at his desk half in shadow. Good music; this movie may do for Beethoven's "Pathetique" sonata what THE LADYKILLERS did for Boccherini's "Minuet." Many striking moments although the Italian wedding sequence with the guy riding a pig seemed a bit much, especially right after McDormand's snide comments about "the wops." (They made a point about period prejudice, then seemed to indulge in it themselves.) In short, a lot of vintage Coen, BUT.... There was just nothing here to care about. The protagonist had drifted into being a barber; okay fine, that was his choice. Married a woman with whom he had no rapport, again his choice. This was no personification of postwar America or modern existentialist man or what have you, just some semi-dim bulb who took what life gave him &amp; seemed attached to nothing in life but his cigarettes. (He even smoked while cutting hair; even in the 1940s would customers have put up with that?) He makes several decisions (necessary for the plot) that seem patently ludicrous. The trademark Coen ironic plot twists have no sort of ring of inevitability to them, they're just clever movie plot twists. The scene with the girl in the car was jaw-droppingly out of the blue, related to nothing happening beforehand or afterwards, just seemed added as another Noir reference. I won't "spoil" the ending, mainly 'cause there's really nothing to spoil, it seems as desultory as everything before it, but I'll just say----SPOILER IF YOU'VE READ CAMUS' "THE STRANGER"----it really doesn't hold a candle to Camus' "The Stranger," just more vacuous ramblings by a vacuous guy sleepwalking through life. Man who wasn't there? This was the movie that wasn't there. Sometimes less really is less. But of course the Coens are geniuses &amp; all that. By the way---since the movie's elephantine pace gave me time to notice stuff like this---in one scene where McDormand wears glasses, we can tell they're not prescription lenses 'cause there's no refraction through them. Seemed an odd slip, given the general attention to detail. Maybe it was deliberate, see if someone would notice? Finally, the movie's title is itself another Noir reference, from the scene in DETECTIVE STORY (1950) where the detectives are ridiculing Joseph Wiseman's alibi, until William Bendix chortles "The man who wasn't there..." Still &amp; all, worth a look for the acting &amp; decor. It was literally the only movie I could find to want to see this weekend, although I would've preferred to see MULHOLLAND DRIVE again if it were still playing....</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2001-12-07</date>
		<username>Awesomizer</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Excellent use of lighting</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Lighting becomes extremely important when a movie is filmed in black and white. The director is given the responsibility to decide how to light the scene and which parts to highlight. Joel Coen appeared to really enjoy this responsibility with his very unique and interesting use of lighting techniques. I especially enjoyed a scene in a dark cell where only a beam of light shined in from a window that was enclosed in bars. The stark boundary of the light cut actor Tony Shalhoub under his eyes, forcing the audience's attention to his smug grin. The boundary wouldn't have been so stark and the emphasis would have been lost if the film was done in color.I realize it's a small detail, but attention to detail is what separates the great movies from the good movies.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Extraordinary Movie About an Ordinary Man</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I absolutely loved this movie. It is the tale of a man so ordinary, so passive, whose one uncharacteristic act sets in motion a chain of events that affects everyone around him! Billy Bob Thornton, who is fast becoming my favorite actor, plays barber Ed Crane without a smile. His wife Doris (Frances McDormand) is a bookkeeper at Nirdlinger's department store. (Don't you just love that name?) James Gandolfini is her boss. He's a big blustery guy who is fond of regaling folks with tales of his WWII heroics against "the Nips." And he's married into the family that owns the store. Ed suspects that Doris and Big Dave (Gandolfini) are having an affair, but he really doesn't care. He breaks out of his ennui, only when a sleazy salesman comes to town looking for financial backing for a chain of dry cleaning stores. Ed bites at the possibility of a better life, but the route he takes to get there proves deadly. Beautifully acted, carefully shot in velvety black and white, The Man Who Wasn't There is a modern film noir masterpiece from Ethan and Joel Coen. Their 1949 Santa Rosa, California, is perfect. The costumes are perfect. Everyone wears a hat. And everyone, except the kids, smokes! You haven't seen this much smoke in a film since. . . .well, since I can't remember. It's my favorite film of 2001. Go see it, then let's talk!</fullReview>

		<date>2001-11-30</date>
		<username>Glida</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>T.S. Elliot meets "Out of the Past" in a barber shop</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen brothers new release, The Man Who Wasn't There, contains some of the most imaginative black and white images I've seen in awhile. Even though the plot and performances were quite effective, I was really less captivated by the discourse of the film than its look. TMWWT was originally shot in color, and then released in black in white with the texture of a color film with the color turned all the way down. The result is a washed out effect, which becomes a visual metaphor for the emptiness of the characters, particularly the protagonist Ed Crane, played with subtle and slight expression (almost excruciating understatement) by Billy Bob Thornton in an Oscar worthy performance. The noirish use of light and shadow reminded me of von Sterberg's "Morroco," (a descendent of German Expressionism) as well as the noir films of the '40s and '50s. The visual style was not only a great homage to the genre, but also unified the film material. Roger Deakins has shot a number of films for the Coens, and this certainly is first rate. Carter Burwell's score, as always, helps support the mood, and blends well with the film's look. While in many ways typical of the genre, the film itself atypically works at a very deliberate pace. Of course, the Coen's typically walk a shifting line between tragedy and satire (ref. "Barton Fink"), where here the languorous pacing turns the tale toward the surreal both in tone and, in at least one occasion, content. Ed Crane (Thornton) is the second barber working in his brother-in-law's (Michael Badalucco) shop. Ed is married to Doris (Frances McDormand), whom he discovers through attention to the interactional patterns with her boss (James Gandolfini) during a dinner party at their home is having an affair. Soon afterwards at the shop, he serves a customer (Jon Polito) who presents him with a business opportunity requiring 10k to join as a silent partner. The combination of this venture and his wife's infidelity sets in motion the expected double crosses, twists, betrayals which works not only at the level of plot but also at a psychological and relational level.  In a sense, Crane is the embodiment of Elliot's "Hollow Man," "gesture without motion....headpiece filled with straw, alas." There is one telling scene where Ed is peering into the bathroom where his wife is bathing. The background is in focus, while Ed is smoking (always smoking), standing in full silhouette in the foreground standing outside and leaning on the doorway. He comes into the room, and at her command, picks up a razor and shaves her legs, mechanically dipping it into the water, and removes the lather he had just placed from her legs. Here, he seems without person, without substance; a robotic drone with no soul. This scene is repeated metaphorically during a key moment towards the end of the film, which both unifies the work, while demonstrating the eviscerating effect of events on the life of this deeply empty man. The Coen brothers have created one of the most vivid depictions of anomie in several years. TMWWT makes my list of one of the year's best. ***1/2 from me. </fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>This film transcends description</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Like all beauty, this film transcends description, but I'll try it anyway. The thing that hits me with the Coen's films are how they seem to always break the tired trend of predictable characters and, instead, choose to set them in an exhausting world, one that they are completely unaware of. Thorton's character is surrounded by a mundane world and passively chooses to accept his role in that world -- not caring in any way about it or any one, and having no idea as to who he actually is. That is what makes this film so amazing. We are forced to look beyond what he is feeling, simply because he feels nothing, and are left with a moving picture of tranquility that is exemplified by his admirable indifference. I could go into each scene, but, like I wouldn't be doing it justice; see this film if you haven't already. And if you have, see it again, you'll enjoy it even more than you did the first time. By far, the best film of the last few years.</fullReview>

		<username>krosaen</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>An insight into the 50's, Coen Bro's style (slight spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The overall affect of this move was to satirize the 1950s as a time where men had little control over there own life beyond the lot they were given to them at birth. This point was made effectively while keeping me interested with strong characters, and humorous dialogue.What struck me immediately during the movie was how effectively the setting was established; it looked like a 1950's Time magazine advertisement come to life. This is probably due largely to Roger Deakins' cinematography efforts, but the wardrobes, cars, props, even the 4 or 5 standard hair cuts that Ed Crane gives day after day (flat top, executive contour etc.) all contributed. The setting let you know that the characters are in a different time where things are much more uniform and cut and dry than they are today.The plot was similar to The Big Lebowski's in that it wasn't the most important part; if you try to follow it very closely, and look for the strength of the movie there, you might be disappointed. It basically takes an ordinary man, and puts him in extraordinary circumstances. There are parts where explanation is left out (the 2nd trial), and parts where the viewer is left wondering the legitimacy (why they wouldn't fingerprint the murder weapon).These minor flaws aside, the plot served to have Ed Crane lose control over his life the minute he attempted to take control of it (by blackmailing for money to start a business).The characters were what ultimately made the movie for me. As often the case in Coen Bro's movies (especially the Hudsucker Proxy), many of the characters were characterizations of their role in life. That is to say, certain aspects of characters where amplified to serve the satirizing effect of the movie. This makes them seem unrealistic and zany, but it is very effective at making the point of the movie stronger and at making the charicters interesting. Ed Crane is soooo ordinary, and his life soooo routine and boring. The lawyer, Freddy Riedenschneider, is soooo self-centered and focused on winning. When Ed Crane tells him that he murdered big Dave, Freddy simply dismisses it as a bad defense case and starts thinking of other plans of defense without stopping for a second to consider the truth of what Ed has just said.Most importantly, the movie kept me interested, and entertained. I felt sorry for Ed, but at the same time found his observations in the spot of a helpless man very interesting.9/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2001-11-25</date>
		<username>RolloTomasi</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The Coens grow up (almost)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Man Who Wasn't There" is the most subdued of the Coens' films and the longest. They've always seemed to design their work to please today's attention span-deficient audiences. Their latest, though, takes a few moments to look around. It breathes. The film runs only two hours but feels longer. This may be the Coens' most thoughtful, sophisticated film yet. It's many things: a comment on the post-WWII era, the economy, flying saucers, and cigarettes (Notice how almost everyone smokes and does so in almost every scene).It's a good story but meanders sometimes aimlessly in true Coen fashion. Once again, it feels as if they made it up as they went along. But if this writing technique has worked well before, why not keep using it? The story always finds itself eventually. "Man" is your basic normal-guy-in-extraordinary-circumstances story. Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton) acts on instinct and doesn't seem to care what the consequences are for his actions. Everything works out well for him as long as no one believes that this common barber could be capable of such mischief. The performances are as usual second to none, especially by Thornton. The film, like its main character, is a contradiction: it's their second weirdest picture behind "Barton Fink" and yet it's also their most mainstream. The brothers are regarded as indie filmmakers but their films consistently have a slick, mainstream look. This is due in no small part to who they've hired to shoot their films, Barry Sonenfeld at first and then later (starting with "Fink") Roger Deakins. I'm not convinced this should have been transferred to black and white. The studio wouldn't let them shoot in B&amp;W so they compromised: shoot in color, transfer it to a black and white print. That way a color print is still available (an inevitable feature on the ?special edition' DVD, I'm sure). I don't like the way their compromise looks, though. It feels like cheating. You can tell it wasn't shot in black and white. I wish they would have held fast and gotten the blessing to shoot it on B&amp;W film. As it is, I can only imagine how much better Deakins' photography would've looked.The Coens have earned the kind of reverence in the filmmaking community that has Academy members and critics looking for an excuse to heap awards on them. "Man" will end up being another such excuse. You could make an argument for it in almost any Oscar category and it's likely to capture a few nominations. The Academy is looking for an excuse to award Deakins and this is probably it. They love Thornton and McDormand as well. I don't see any way this doesn't get an original screenplay nomination either.Grade: A-</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Breaking the Rules</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There are certain rules to moviemaking, as there are to all art forms.First of all, the art must be entertaining, for it is not, the public will not be pleased. And even the final effect of experiencing the art must be satisfying, for even today the greatest marketing tool at anyone's disposal is "word of mouth".It is not enough, for example, to be pretentious and to concoct a really suicidal scenario, for the public will leave the cinema yucking their tongues, not at all happy, and then if and when their friends ask them if the movie is worth seeing, their countenances will tell all. (There are pretentious people in this world who really think they have something if they partake of pretentious art, but fortunately they are in the far minority.)Secondly and continually afterwards, any other rule you want to apply to moviemaking - or again any other art form - is stillborn if the first rule is broken. Do you have something important to say? Then let it be conveyed on the carrier wave of good entertainment. Many great comedians are great social critics, and the reason they can influence people is that they make people laugh - the public is entertained.This movie comes with the reputation of these brother from Texas, their somewhat uneven portfolio that on the whole is still very good, and that's what drives the few who see this movie to actually see it. But the majority of these people are, in my opinion, sorely disappointed, for the rather egotistical Coen Brothers have disregarded the First Rule of Art.This was the first of two movies I saw on the same day, something I do not do very often, only when the first cinematic experience is so BAD a new and better cinematic experience is needed immediately. I was fortunate enough to get in to see Spy Games only a few hours later, and was much more pleased with that movie, and finally could go home with a smile on my face.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Coens are back on form!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is simply a modern 'noir' masterpiece with brilliant performances all round, especially from Billy Bob Thornton as Ed Crane and Frances McDormand as his wife Alice.Ed Crane is a laconic, small town barber and an outwardly unexceptional man, lacking in passion and stuck in a dreary life and sexless marriage. Laconicness is often a quality we associate with careful, wise thinking and circumspection. In reality Ed Crane is both hapless and devious (like Jerry Lundeegard in 'Fargo'). Crane cons his wife's boss, department store manager Big Dave Nirdlinger, played by James Gandolfini, (with whom she is having an affair) into handing over blackmail money to a con artist whose spurious business idea he had initially refused. Things come full circle when we learn that Big Dave has found out from the confidence trickster (with the help of his fists) about Ed's sneaky little trick. In a frenzied fight, during an after hours meeting at Nirdlingers', Ed kills Big Dave in self-defence. The authorities discovery of the embezzled money leads to a murder charge being made against Alice and from then on Ed's foolishness and deceit leads to greater tragedy.A strong theme in Coen brothers' films is how ordinary people's attempts to find shortcuts out of humble or uncomfortable predicaments ends up creating uncontrollable mayhem and disaster. This is a superb film, beautifully shot in black-and-white and compelling right to the end. This could be the best ever Coen brothers' movie, and there's a long line of great films to choose from.</fullReview>

		<username>pulpbricktion</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Great Metaphor</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Waiting to see this movie, I had to wonder how it would compare to Fargo. Probably my favorite thing about Fargo was how intensely white everything was, symbolizing the almost sinister benevolence of Fargo towards the hapless people who lived there. The Man Who Wasn't There's big metaphor is just as fun; hair as a symbol of growth. Noteworthy is the fact that Ed Crane's head of hair is the only one that keeps growing throughout the picture. People go to him to get their hair cut; people in general don't want to grow, and it foreshadows him as someone who'd take a life. The traveling salesman is both bald and has an unrealistically full head of hair, suggesting both his supposed amoral behavior in skipping town with the money and his being surprisingly innocent of that offense. The list just goes on. I highly recommend seeing this movie, then seeing it again.</fullReview>
