		<username>MBunge</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>It won't make you a fan of the Coen Brothers if you aren't already one</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Man Who Wasn't There is like a beautiful porcelain doll you are not allowed to touch. It's nice to look at, but you can't have any fun with it.Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton) is an unhappy stoic. He talks in a monotone. He thinks in a monotone. He lives in a monotone. We know this not just because we can see it in all this film's black and white glory. We also hear it as Ed narrates this story of his life in the 1950s. He tells us how he married his wife Doris (Frances McDormand) and became the 2nd chair barber at the shop of his brother-in-law Frank (Michael Badalucco). He even tells us about the affair he suspects his wife of having with her boss, Big Dave Brashear (James Gandolfini). Ed feels himself sinking into the quicksand of his existence and he doesn't even have the will to try and claw his way out.Then one day, Ed sees that way out. For 10 thousand dollars he could buy into the dry cleaning dreams of a traveling entrepreneur named Creighton Tolliver (Jon Polito). To get the money, Ed decides to blackmail Big Dave. He anonymously demands Big Dave give him 10 thousand dollars or the secret of his affair with Doris will get out. Big Dave agrees to pay but like just about every blackmail scheme in a black and white movie, things go wrong and people end up dead. Both Doris and Ed end up on trial for murder, forcing them to mortgage their lives to pay big city defense attorney Freddie Reidenschnieder (Tony Shalhoub). In the midst of all this, Ed finds himself drawn to young Rachel Abundas (Scarlett Johansson) and her magical playing of the piano. Ed finds another way out of his life through her but just like the blackmail scheme, it doesn't turn out anything like he wanted.Like most Cohen brothers movies, this movie is smart, stylish, filled with striking imagery and clever performances and nothing goes quite the way you expect. Like many Cohen brothers movies, it's also not that overtly entertaining. The Man Who Wasn't There is a good demonstration of the difference between brilliant filmmakers like the Cohen brothers and brilliant filmmakers like Stephen Spielberg. The latter are always trying to entertain the audience. The former only try to entertain themselves. Every so often, a Cohen brothers movie will resonate with a broader audience but, usually, their storytelling is something you can admire and appreciate more than you enjoy.That's the case with this film. There's some crackerjack work done by this cast, with Billy Bob Thornton making even the narratively static Ed an interesting presence. The camera work proves again that a well lit black and white movie looks a lot better than a color film. The direction balances a leisurely, mesmerizing pace with an awful lot of activity. There're even a few touches of the Cohen brother's trademarked oddness.In the end, though, this remains a movie for folks who like watching the Cohens entertain themselves. There's a passivity and distance to the story that may have been a deliberate creative choice, but it's still unappealing. You never know where the film is going, but you never care where it ends up.If you're a Cohen brothers fan, you've probably already seen The Man Who Wasn't There. If you're not, I don't think watching this movie will turn you into one.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Coens trade humor for perfection</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A movie that has everything going for it except entertainment. An unemotional man's search for life's meaning has the Coen brothers weaving together some of the key existential questions in a well-constructed story. The movie is cinematically perfect, replete with great acting, brilliant direction and engaging cinematography. However, the one thing I find missing from the movie is entertainment that I associate with most of the Coens' offerings. After all, these are the directors who filmed a melancholy story like Fargo in a charmingly funny way. In this movie, the Coens leave behind attempts at dark humor to portray a consistently grim picture. The scenes are long-drawn at times, sometimes to the point of being boring. While the movie illustrates the directorial abilities of the Coens, the morose and slow nature of the story means that a viewer needs to be in a certain mood to enjoy the film. Overall, I think the movie, especially the script, is an advertisement for Coens' skills, but in being drab, it is one of the few films from the brothers where you will not think and enjoy at the same time.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I'm a big fan of the Coen brother's other work and so I was really looking forward to this movie. The rave reviews and the mysteriously low-key release schedule (here in the US anyway) just added to my enthusiasm. After sitting in the movie theater for a couple of hours with this film however I was just keen to leave. The story plods along in an admittedly odd but hardly riveting fashion, and, just like most of the supporting characters in the film, you don't really care what happens to the main character anymore. Making a movie about a complete non-entity with seemingly no interest in the world he lives in, may have seemed like an interesting concept, I guess, but it makes for pretty uninteresting watching.Frankly if I wanted to watch a movie about a moron to whom weird things happen through no fault of his own, I'd probably go see Forrest Gump. Maybe...</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>(contains minor spoilers)The Coen brothers' new movie is very uneven, and in its unevenness it is ultimately disappointing. The problem as I see it is entirely with the script; the actors are all great (Billy Bob Thornton is excellent as Ed Crane) and do their best to deal with a script that doesn't know where it's going.The Man Who Wasn't There starts as a film noir; the problem is that none of the characters is sufficiently noir. Ed Crane may be bad enough to blackmail somebody, but he is not really a murderer, and he's a far cry from the ultimate good guy turned bad, Fred MacMurray's Walter Neff from Double Indemnity. Not happy with the homage to the film noir, the script was spiced up with an obvious nod to Stanley Kubrick's Lolita, which I felt was totally out of place and simply added 30 superfluous minutes to a movie that was at any rate too long.I call this a major disappointment. 6/10, and that's only for the actors and the cinematography. The script may be among the Coens' weakest so far.</fullReview>

		<date>2001-11-30</date>
		<username>Scott Strickland (jstrick1@rochester.rr.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Postmodernism takes on another topic in Coens' new tour de force</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen brothers are the outstanding example of postmodern film making. While other artists play around with the trappings of postmodernism, Joel and Ethan eschew most of the glitz and go straight to the heart of the aesthetic approach. More than anything else, what they do is always playful, inviting audiences to engage in word play, thematic expropriation, overlaid images, and, most expertly, the game of guess-what-I'm-trying-to-pull-on-you-now. Some of their contemporaries make narrative more obscure, turn and twist sound to fracture perception, brutalize the viewer with violent acts or emotions, propagate vertigo with hand-held cameras and digital images, and exploit the full bang of their technical armories. Sometimes the affect is profound and lasting; and sometimes the result is thoroughly confusing. I can think of a respectable collection of movies in 2001 that have burrowed into me and remained unsettling for some time after I've left the theater. I never feel that way with the Coens' films. However, no one entertains and stimulates me intellectually like the Coens, and THE MAN. . . is no exception.Last year's unexcelled O BROTHER. . . traded in the ODYSSEY, as has been well acknowledged. Far less discussed was its use of THE WIZARD OF OZ, of Busby Berkeley extravaganzas, of dance-number movies of the '40s and '50s, of WPA documentary photography, particularly that of Walker Evans, and of the American classic film of the '30s, THE GRAPES OF WRATH. Joel and Ethan ran rampant through the "arts" stealing this and that at will and making a beautiful pastiche that truly worked. Plus it had the great music to hold it all together. This time they've turned even more unexpectedly to the somber existentialism of the post-WWII West, particularly Camus; to "Lonely Crowd" and "Organization Man" alienation; and, with my greatest surprise, theoretical physics. Who would have ever thought that someone could make a movie with its core built around Heisenberg's Uncertainty Principle (even if it's oversimplified)? I can't wait to see my astrophysicist friend at a folk music performance this weekend and tell him the Coens have made another movie right down his alley. Then there are all the tangents that add to the entertainment. When the Coens put Heisenberg at the center and began the film with the observation that the Russians had the atomic bomb, were they referring to the controversy over whether or not the great thinker actively prevented the Nazis from developing their own?Saying more would ruin the movie for those who haven't seen it. If you are among them and like the Coens, go see it. But be prepared to be teased, tricked, and tickled, often all at the same time. Wow, it's great.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>It is Ok..</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So another Coen film eh? The noir movie season continues with the greatness of Memento and Mulholland Drive, now we have a black and white 50's potrayal of Ed Crane involved in a most twisted plot. Ed (Billy Bob) is a barber who in short, is a sap. He doesn't talk much, is anti-social, has a wife who uses him more out of convenience than anything cause he makes such a good door mat. Ed's life is pretty uneventful. Until one day a stranger comes to down (a con artist) that tries to get Ed to invest 10,000 dollars in a scheme. Since Ed is so sad and even has the intelligence to know that the guy seems to be a crook, he decides to get him the money anyway, as if to make his life more meaningful or perhaps interesting. But how he gets the money creates a cascade of events that is well written and devised.Ed is played with great realism by Billy Bob. How he holds his smokes, how his delayed voice takes time to draw out, is all handled masterfully. There's even a great scene where the con artist makes a pass at Ed by winking at him, and Ed's only reaction is to flash his eyes in surprise. The look on Billy Bob's face is so simple and uninvolved but conveys a message so well with the littlest of acting whatsoever. A real testament to Thornton's ability. The Coens once again create an ingenious script, but this time it takes a little more patience to sit through. There also the typical dark humour situated with Coen films such as Fargo but nowhere near the greatness of previous work..I did find myself at one point in the film giving up on it, but it brought itself back when the actors got there chance to show off, and key things started happening. Ed's wife played by Frances McDormand is wonderful, and the best acting is even reserved for the lawyer Freddy Riedenschneider (Tony Shalhoub), who plays his part with such zeal you wonder if there is even an actor on the screen. But what I enjoyed most from this film was its look. It is probably the best looking picture I have seen all year, using just the perfect black and white tone, and creating some awesome work using light, shading, shadows, and cigarette smoke. It also has the great nostalgic feel. It's not a great film, but has some real good performances, and looks amazing. I would still recommend it even so, but I wouldn't exactly run to theatre to see it again either.Rating 7 out of 10.</fullReview>

		<date>2001-11-08</date>
		<username>bluesdoctor</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>there isn't any there here</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What to do when sophistication precludes conviction, self-consciousness, credibility? What to do when the musical accompaniment to a movie has more meaning than the movie itself?This is a pretentious movie, technically accomplished, stylistically complete, but hollow at its core; it's facility without feeling. The Coen's strain for the metaphysical within the limited confines of noire pulp, but because they violate the basic defining premises of their chosen genre, break its rigid rules, they fail. This is a highly embellished but essentially simple sordid tale of infidelity, blackmail, suicide, and murder that begs The Big Questions: Who Are we? What is life? It looks for meaning in a grain of sand but sees only sand.The look of the movie, a lustrous black &amp; white, is flawless, its visual composition, studied (there's an overhead shot, all shadow and light, of the main character exiting a building that must be a reference to Edward Hopper's etching "Night Shadows"). The Coen's are masters of the simple dramatic gesture, of buried body language. It's obvious that a couple are having an affair by the way they sit next to each other at a table, even in the presence of their spouses. And the Coen's do violence extremely well: A knife to the neck flashes by for a fraction of a second, obscenely savage and unforgettable. A small-town retro-'50's setting is meant to expose essential Americana, like a Hopper painting. Hip modern minimalism enhances irony, understatement, drama. But the deadpan dry black humor the Coen's do so well ("Fargo") is unfortunately restricted. There is too much control: their usual sensitivity to language and idiom becomes mannered; there is an anal preoccupation with, a stringent tyranny over the physical: Cars are spotless, interiors immaculately arranged, clothes too right, nothing breathes. This banishment of chaos is one with their devout adherence to stifling noire convention. The movie puts itself in a straight jacket, but then attempts ballet. It does away with psychological reality, the fuss and muss of the irrational; there is no character, only caricature. Its uptight, leaden, literal physicality eliminates the openness needed for multiple meanings and symbolism. When here and there, of a sudden, it probes below its meticulously contrived, brilliant but brittle surface to explore the depths below, that surface shatters. When the principal, a barber, takes to pondering the relentless regenerative onslaught of life, he thinks in terms of how hair keeps on growing on people's heads of its own, only for him to endlessly cut it off. It's as if Bogart's Sam Spade were to stop at a pier self-consciously made to look like a Hollywood set and ponder how time flows like a river. The Coen's violate the convention they labor so punctiliously to establish, undercutting both it and the meaning they're looking for beyond it. The effect is trite, worse, untrue to formImprobabilities, holes abound--not good when plot is so paramount: Why does the adulterer confide in the adulteree about being blackmailed? Fingerprints on the murder weapon left at the scene of the crime are completely overlooked. The murder cases made against each Crane, husband and wife, are wholly circumstantial, insufficient to bring to trial. The wrong persons accused twice is simply too (pun) much. The suicide is poorly motivated. The fate of the $10,000 should have been made clear at the onset, prior to the discovery of the body. The girl's sudden sexual solicitude is pure male fantasy.The soundtrack depends on small quotes from several Beethoven piano sonatas as the only illumination in an otherwise dreary world; yet, inadvertently, the artistic freedom, honesty, and courage embodied by that music, even the atypically morose and simple portions quoted, only serve to underscore the lack of these very things in the movie itself. The girl who plays the sonatas is taken to a respected music teacher to see if he will take her as his pupil. His verdict is that she is a "nice" girl, too "nice," that she plays the right notes but doesn't have a feeling for the deeper meanings of the music. So too this movie. It is impressively accurate and true to form, downright punctiliously precise, but bereft of soul.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2001-11-07</date>
		<username>Patrick Brogan</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>The Invisible Man.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Not since the stroke of luck by Steve Soddenberg that he had last year (and hoping again on December 7th!) that a director has done two excellent movies back to back, and having both films released within a year. Spielberg has done it, so has John Carpenter, now Joel and Ethan Coen return to the big screen for the first time since their last highly successful hit O BROTHER WHERE ART THOU? with what is perhaps one of their best films in their careers with THE MAN WHO WASN'T THERE. MAN WHO WASN'T THERE is about a man who most of the time seems like he isn't there, Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton). Ed is a simple looking man for 1949, with his Sinatra hair-stlye, smoking a cigarette, and giving young boys haircuts with different types of style, you would think that he is boring and harmless. That's on the outside, inside Ed there's a brilliant and intelligent man who is aware that his wife Doris (Frances McDormand) is having an affair with her department store boss, Big Dave Brewster (James Gandolfini). And when Ed is proposed by a smooth-talking salesman Creighton Tolliver (Jon Polito), to co-fiance a dry cleaning business and become partners, Ed figures that the easiest way to come up with $10,000 is to blackmail Big Dave by sending him a anomouous letter. Big Dave is furious and when he asks Ed to come to Big Dave's department store, Dave suspects Ed for the blackmail and tries to put a stop to this blackmailing, thus leaving Ed to do the most reasonable action, defend himself, thus killing Big Dave. Ironic, it's not Ed being charged with Dave's murder but Doris! This has Ed's attorney and friend Walter Abundas (Richard Jenkins) to suggest that Ed hires flashy attorney Freddy Riedenschneider (Tony Shalhoub). Freddy is the 1940's Eddie Cochran or F. Lee Bailey, the type of attorney that would make Charles Manson look like a misuderstood fella. During the lead up to the trial, Ed takes over the barber shop while his brother-in-law and boss Frank Raffo (Michael Badalucco) figures out his fiances to support Freddy's charge of defense, and Ed begins a passion affection for Walter's piano playing daughter Birdy (Scarlett Johansson). If all of this seems complex and unusual, it is, it's a Coen brothers film of course. Which means that if you are a Coen brothers fan (like myself) then MAN WHO WASN'T THERE is a major must see! I would rank this film to be one of their best films, (behind FARGO and BLOOD SIMPLE). This is a amazing film with the different techniques that the Coen brothers use to tell this story. First there is the cinematography by Coen regular Roger Deakins. Deakins uses only black and white for the coloring of the film, and some scenes (including the ending) are spectacular with the image and coloring that the film shows. I also loved how Deakins would have Ed look outside his automobile window as the car is driving slow, and to see the rapid and crazy rush of the people walking down the streets of Santa Rosiata, California. Seocnd there is the acting, I thought Billy Bob Thornton did a great job in BANDITS. In MAN WHO WASN'T THERE, he blows his previous acting job out of the water with what is perhaps his best role since SLING BLADE. Ed doesn't say much in the film, he narrates the story. Imagine that quiet and lonely man sitting at the coffee shop table reading the newspaper, what is he thinking? With Ed you hear and follow everything that is on his mind, and makes Ed one of the most intresting characters to narrate a story since Edward Norton's Narratror "Jack" character from FIGHT CLUB. Plus, I also enjoyed the acting by the always wonderful Frances McDormand who does a good job playing the grouchy wife of the quiet barber, and James Gandolfini does a great job as well as the bullying Big Dave. But the only actor that almost steals the show from Thronton is Tony Shalhoub as the oily defense attorney. Shalhoub does a excellent job describing his plans and possible motives to help give the accussed Doris a acquittial. There's even a hilarious scene where Ed actually confesses to the murder, and Freddy says that "the guilty husband motive won't work." Third there is the music by another regular Carter Burwell. The music for this film is like Ed, quiet and simple. Burwell uses mostly piano solos including a piece from Beethoven, for the background of the movie. There are times when some films work with big and flashy composing scores, like scores by John Williams and Danny Elfman. Then there are those others that can be just as effective by being timid and soft, Burwell succeeds with this film. And finally, there is the Coen Brothers, the best sibling team filmmakers in Hollywood (yes, they are better than the "only made two films" Wicholski brothers). They are masters at story telling, one film would be a dark murder mystery, the next a wacked out crazy comedy, then a dark comic murder thriller, to a bizare and funny comedy/musical. The Coen Brothers have made a variety of style of films, and they richly deserved to win the Best Director's Award for the 2001 Cannes Film Festival (David Lynch also gladly deserved to win as well, but that's a different story). I feel that if the Academy gets their heads out of the clouds and into reality, they would finally recognize the directing duo with a award for Best Director and even Best Picture for this film, for this is another showcase of their brilliant work. THE MAN WHO WASN'T THERE is a film that will be like most of the Coen Brothers films, some will love it, others will despise it. I for one, love all of the Coen Brothers films, and I will say that this film, is one that should be put on the must see list of their films as #2, only behind FARGO, and that's a great achievement! You'll be a darn fool to miss this film! ***** (out of five)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Okay, I admit to being overly forgiving of the shortcomings of a lot of motion pictures. The pace reminded me of winter molasses races where the spectators all leave before the finish. Pace should be a factor of story; only if the story is boring should the pace bore you also, so I must admit it fit. There was the promise of a story, but we never really got to the story of this barber, of how he didn't really have a life, but only existed in the same way a zero holds a place in a number-- maybe because the zero is important, and it has meaning. Ed, it appears, did not, and no matter what winks, nods, and nudges employed, we never really saw into Ed, we never saw why his life was empty, what role he had in the community, in family, or in anything. To tell the story of a nothing you have to tell the story of something, and they did not.We make a lot of jokes about sugar-free meaning flavor free, but in this case truth in advertising would lead to a tag line of "Beautiful look with half the screenplay and TWICE the tedium!" Who knows? It might sell tickets. Some people enjoy being bored.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Potentially Good Film Noir Goes Horribly Off Track</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The black and white cinematography. The voice over narration. The black mail. The murder. It's all here, so what goes wrong?The Coen brothers have the right style, but they're not sure what to do with it. Everything is just right up until the murder (I'm being vague out of consideration), but then the movie gets bogged down with the lawyer and his investigation. The subplot involving the girl and the piano just did not belong in the same film. I suppose I appreciated the way the movie tried to come around at the end by making Thornton's character pay for an one death after getting away with causing another (a la "The Postman Always Rings Twice"), but it takes so long to get there that it just doesn't have any kind of impact. And don't even get me started about the UFO. Yeah, that's right. The UFO.Noir's simple. You take an aimless man, have him carry out a desperate crime, and then watch him squirm as the walls start closing in. You keep it simple. I felt angry and cheated after "The Man Who Wasn't There" was over. My girlfriend suggested that the film wasn't so much a film noir as an *homage* to the film noir style. Maybe, but who wants to see that? Noir is my favorite of all genres. It is a genre so rarely made today that when someone does get together the right look, characters, and situations, I want to see it done right.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Coen's dreamy nightmare</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>THE MAN WHO WASN'T THERE (2001) *** Billy Bob Thornton, Frances McDormand, James Gandolfini, Michael Badalucco, Tony Shalhoub, Richard Jenkins, Scarlett Johansson, Jon Polito, Katherine Borowitz, Christopher McDonald. The Coen Brothers' pulpy fiction salute to James M. Cain focuses on dour barber Thornton (in an excellent turn here) circa 1949 whose nowhere existence takes a turn for the better/worse when he decides to invest in a dry cleaning business with shady goings on and a serpentine turn into a web of film noir deceit and homicide. Gorgeously lensed by premiere cinematographer Roger Deakins, who filmed in color and processed into richly textured, silvery black and white, giving the film a lustrous tone to the somber proceedings with a ghostly pallor. Strong supporting cast and a decidedly leisurely paced direction combine to keep the viewer focused to see what else is going on in the story at hand. Craftsmanship filmmaking indeed.</fullReview>

However I must say when I first sat down and put the film on (I made the oft made mistake of putting a film that requires your full attention on whilst in the middle of doing various other things therefore not delivering my full attention) I found it cold and difficult to engage with. The cinematography is stunning and the beauty of the simple but effective slow paced shots with long shadows and lingering smoke kept me interested for a while but I could not engage with the dry conversation and seemingly low pace of the plot development to begin with. The main protagonist is quiet by his own admission and it seemed that his voice-overs were the only real plot driver (I had other things to do and twice didn't get past the first 40 minutes) However I now admit that I was completely wrong about this film and it took a night where I was too tired to move off the sofa and finally gave this film the attention it deserved to really appreciate it. There are strong characters and a slow developing but ultimately gripping set of plots and themes behind the initially cold dialogue and beautiful shots. At times it is surprising, sometimes shocking and on occasion darkly comic but once you have watched this film properly it will get under your skin in the same manner as many of the best coen films and you will spend time pondering its motifs.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Solid but no classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen's finally try to do a noir. They've already done Preston Sturges and Screwball Comedies and have a thing for paying homage to movies from a bygone era. Only the Sopranos guy and Tony Chalhoub look the part in their roles. The second problem is the dialogue. There is not one memorable one-liner thrown in. No wisecracks either and that is one thing a person always remembers from a noir film - the dialogue. Dialogue from 40's movies is quoted even today. Here its only meant to service the plot - no zing, no bitchiness.</fullReview>

		<username>he_who_leads</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>I Don't Mind Slowly Paced or Quietly Meaningful - But This is Plain Dull</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This film is boring. Boring Boring Boring. And this is coming from someone who usually appreciates the Coen Brothers' movies. This from someone who has seen 'The English Patient' about three times and is planning to watch it again some day. This from someone who shakes their head when they hear people refusing to watch black and white films.When asked what this film is about, the Coens responded dryly that it is about a barber who wants to be a dry-cleaner. I liked that line. It's so Coen-ish. However, that may as well be the extent of the film. Billy Bob Thornton, a very underrated actor, pulls off the role of Ed Crane - a man who isn't really there. He has no real drive or passion for life. He just exists and watches those around him consume life in big gulps - his wife (Frances McDormand), her boss (Gandolfini), his brother in law (Badalucco) etc. Ed just watches these people go about their business like ants running around getting caught up in their own minor little worlds to no avail. Though I knew I was meant to side with Ed, I didn't. His detached passivity pervades the whole film like noxious gas. There are many great films about detached, alienated, underachieving protagonists. Yet this film, through Ed, successfully suffocates everything around him. I found myself trying to cling to the little pleasures - snippets of dialogue, the cinematography, Coen regular Jon Polito - but to no avail. While this film has things to say about life, meaning and perception, they aren't engaging at all and the lack of any cohesiveness is distracting. Time just passes and doesn't go anywhere. Things don't stick. My suggestion? watch other, more worthy Coen pictures instead.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Far less than the sum of its parts.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Coen Brothers are in dire need of intervening help; so dire that they don't think they need any. They let their love for the Screen block any critical self-editing. TMWWT is a series of first acts to oldie-but-goodie film-noir ideas strung together in the apparent hopes of making another Euro-style statement on One Modern Man's Predicament. It merely comes across as what they are: ideas that the Coens don't dare let go for fear they will get away from them. A good many pulp-serials prove to be the same way when they're published in one-volume. The Coens have the right ingredients but they cook them wrong. TMWWT is great moviemaking but it's no movie.</fullReview>

		<date>2004-03-05</date>
		<username>misterpennycandy</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>The critic who didn't care</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Ask yourself one question: Do you feel lucky, Coen?, to paraphrase a line from "Dirty Harry." To say that The Man Who Wasn't There is the first stinker to bear the Coen brothers name is questionable. A lot of people didn't care for Hudsucker Proxy, but it's a lot funnier and a lot more endearing than TMWWT. The Coens are misunderstood. First of all their name is pronounced "Cone" not "Co-hen," seeing as though they're Irish and not Jewish. That's not such a big deal. The fact that critics championed this film as post-noir is somewhat laughable. Like in the aforementioned HUDSUCKER, the Bros. Co-hinn's attempt to emulate an arcane cinematechnique results in an unintentional parody of the genre. While they can produce really good post-noir like Blood Simple and Fargo, TMWWT shows that they may be taking themselves a little too seriously. First of all, the plot relies on a seemingly endless series of unfeasible coincidences. The film itself relies on the viewer to take some pity on Billy Bob Thorton's Barber - an apologetic, monotonous, boring loser whose flirtatious wife, Frances McDormand, is perpetually having an affair. This is impossible because the character is difficult to understand. Thorton's performance makes this even more difficult, since he's so boring. He can't be funny - when he tries to be funny it's sad, and vice versa. Frances McDormand's character, who always seems to be drunk, is involved with Tony Soprano. Tony Soprano's wife is, apparently, insane. Now you know how it goes from here: somebody who's involved in something does something to somebody who's involved with someone who's involved in something somehow relate to the something that someone is doing to someone, and then, the end. Unfortunatley, after all the film-noir parody - yes, film noir parody - is over and done with, there's a tacked on plot with Ms. Lost in Translation in what i would call an unfortunate performance in the sense that she's too modern and too arrogant to be featured in a period piece. Thorton thinks she has a chance of making it, so he emotionally invests himself in ensuring a better life for this girl who wow's him with her ability to note read Beethoven. The courtroom scenes are the height of tedium. Calling this dramedy is a laugh. If you believe the lines "he said I was modern man" are anything other than comedy, you're sadly mistaken. The plot twists and turns so much - it doesn't make sense, and it isn't entertaining. The acting is bad, it's too long, it doesn't work as a comedy. Film noir is just about dark subject matter - sometimes the plots twist a lot, and spellbinds and creates more tension and mystery and confusion - that's what makes it great. Then finally, everything is explained at the end. This is the most unapologetically inarticulate of their genre-parodies. It isn't nearly a comment. It's a plea to a sedate audience that film noir plots should be lampooned for their dense, multi-faceted layers - more films like this should be made! Nothing is sacred anymore! The fact that some movies rely on many stories distilled and jam-packed together into two and 1/2 hours is funny! If this was a straight comedy, it would've worked. It ain't and it don't.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2003-02-19</date>
		<username>MovieAddict2016</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>A Disappointing, Dull Film Noir...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Man Who Wasn't ThereFilm noirs have been done excellently over the past years, but not many are as great as they could be. That's why, after seeing commercials and reading reviews for `The Man Who Wasn't There,' I expected a lot from the film. Unfortunately, it was very disappointing.Ed Crane (Billy Bob Thornton) is second-hand at a barbershop in 1949. He `doesn't talk much,' he says `I just cut the hair.' Well, that's true. Ed Crane barely talks at all. The only thing we hear from him throughout the film is his strangely soft and unmoving voice-over narrative recalling the accounts of the past few weeks of his quiet life.  The story begins when Crane suspects that his wife (Academy Award Winner Frances McDormand) is having an affair with the rich manager of a clothing store, named Big Dave (James Gandolfini). After Crane hears about a dry cleaning scam from a customer, he blackmails Big Dave for ten thousand dollars to invest in the dry cleaning business. Unfortunately, Big Dave soon finds out who the blackmailer is, and in a moment of self-defense, Crane kills Big Dave. Now, Crane's wife has been convicted of murder, and everything in his life goes downhill from there on?`The Man Who Wasn't There' starts off very good. The beginning credits were more than reminiscent of the Humphrey Bogart era, and so was the calm voice-over by Thornton. I was instantly engaged in the story of this man living in a small town in California, and how his life could possibly get any worse. Well, after about an hour, the film had barely moved along at all. The pacing for this movie is very slow, and there are many films that could ? and should ? have been cut not only due to time, but because they paid nothing to the film's progress or character development in any way.Billy Bob Thornton is very good as Ed Crane, and uses the same quiet technique as he did with the character in `Sling Blade.' The movie is worth seeing just for his convincing performance.Frances McDormand is quite believable as his wife; the kind of small-town wife you always hear about. Bingo on Tuesday, alcohol throughout the week; she seems very depressed, and that is probably why she begins to have an affair with Big Dave. James Gandolfini would not be my first choice for the character of Big Dave, but he pulled it off quite nicely. The obese man proves he has more acting talent than just playing Tony Soprano (John Goodman would have made a nice choice for Big Dave, only because he is almost always in the Coen Brothers' films and it seems like they wrote the part for him. I'm surprised they didn't get him for the part ? maybe they tried and he refused?).Tony Shaloub (`Men In Black') plays the most enjoyable role of a big-town lawyer sent in to defend McDormand's character. He is wacky, enjoyable, and his crazy antics make the film. His are the only scenes I really laughed at. It seemed the most like a `Fargo Moment.' The Coen Brothers, creators of `Fargo' and the disappointing `O Brother, Where Art Thou,' wrote and directed this film (well, Joel directed, Ethan produced, they both wrote the script?). I was in the minority about `O Brother, Where Art Thou.' I thought the film was boring, slow-paced and weird (what was the ending all about?). `Fargo' was excellent, mainly because it was demented humor and a great script, with some great actors. This film, however, seems to be boasting `original' all over. It seems the brothers are trying to stuff their recent films with odd bits and pieces to make them original. Well, I didn't find it original.In the end, `The Man Who Wasn't There' is not a winner. It could have been, but after an hour the film seems to slow down to a miserably slow pace, without any good surprises (like no one saw the end coming?). How this got so much attention from the critics is beyond me, because it just seems like one of those `Look at me! I'm original!' films. That's two disappointing Coen Brothers films in a row. For me, at least.2.5/5 stars ? turn the movie off once you get an hour ? 1.5 hours into it?John Ulmer</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Self-consciously dry and offbeat drama of irony has barber Billy Bob Thornton involved with a con-man, a businessman who is having an affair with the barber's wife, and...a spaceship. Black-and-white offering from the hit-and-miss Coen Brothers has a streak of bemused malice, but the pacing is so deliberately slow and languorous that interest wanes in the final stretch. The script is filled with talky speeches that don't propel the plot, which isn't so slyly twisted (as one might expect). Joel and Ethan are certainly talented, and they're full of visual bravado, but the silvery cinematography doesn't reveal anything in particular (it's there to recall old film noir--and to be admired for that alone). Billy Bob Thornton is solid as usual, but this seems an inside joke. *1/2 from ****</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The plot that wasn't there</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Enigmatic title for a typically enigmatic Coen Brothers film, which like the few others of theirs I've seen, sort of passed me by. I think I got it as a homage to film noir, particularly Fritz Lang, referred to at one point by name and in another by the expressionistic lighting used in a prison cell scene, to Hollywood prison dramas and even, bizarrely and briefly, post-war science fiction cinema, although there are some adult scenes which could only belong in a latter-day movie.Told in extended flashback style by Billy-Bob Thornton's quiet man title character from Death Row where he awaits execution (for the wrong murder!), the convolutions of the twisting plot evolve as his dreams of striking it big financially disappear when fate, as it will, takes an unexpected turn.Exquisitely shot in black and white and with many striking images, such as the flying car crash-scene, the spinning tyre hub that morphs into a surgeon's head-light and finally the fade-to-white sequence of Thornton's execution, for me the main problem was the sheer passivity of Thornton's character which didn't engage me at all, plus as ever the tangential nature of the narrative had me flummoxed at times.The acting was suitably apposite to the era but I never doubted for a second that they were being moved about like pawns on a chessboard, Hitchcock-style, to satisfy the director's vision, so that most of the performances seemed one-dimensional to me. I did enjoy the use of Beethoven's piano-music, which certainly added an elegiac air to proceedings, but in the end, film-noir lover as I am, I wasn't sure if this was meant to be a homage or pastiche leaving me puzzled and unfulfilled in the final reckoning.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Slow, depressing, stupid, pointless waste of time... but Billy Bob great as usual.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Here's a lesson in filmmaking one would have thought the Coen Brothers knew by now: great music, dramatic lighting and stellar performances do not a great film make.That's it, plain and simple. All the impeccable craftsmanship one comes to expect from Joel and Ethan and Roger (Deakins) are here, no question about it. Unfortunately they forgot one thing: a story that's actually worth a damn. A reason to make a movie beyond fashioning a nifty black and white film noir just for the hell of it... but that's apparently what they've done."Fargo" was never one of my favorite films... but it's a masterpiece in comparison. It has what this one doesn't: a point. Some sort of reason for being. It leaves the audience with something other than depression. It tells a crime story not unlike this one, but through it we gain insight into some tragic and funny characters. We see desperate people pay the price for their misdeeds. It's basically a crime-doesn't-pay story, but an unusual one seen through an unlikely protagonist: a pregnant police chief who outsmarts everyone the same way Columbo did... unexpectedly. "The Man Who Wasn't There" contains a similar crime-doesn't pay theme... but it rings hollow and flat. As opposed to William H. Macy's character in "Fargo," Ed Crane has no real reason to gain our sympathies... thus we don't really care what happens to him. He's basically dead already... so whether he gets caught or pays for his actions is irrelevant. And compared with Lester Burnham in "American Beauty," he takes no action and makes no change in his life. Lester Burnham started off "dead" too... but he changed. He took action. And that's the difference between a good story -- and sympathetic character -- and "The Man Who Wasn't There."Admittedly, he twists and turns in this film are clever. Deakins's cinematography is genius. The Beethoven sonatas are beautiful. And the entire cast -- including Lolitaesque, classical piano-playing Scarlett Johannson -- is excellent.But is there anyone who leaves the theater after this long, bleak film and NOT ask himself: "What was the point, exactly?"</fullReview>
